The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Definition of Nazar (Reflection): Arranging Knowns to Attain the Unknown
Author defines nazar as arrangement of knowns to attain unknown, with syllogistic illustration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of nazar (reflection) and reconstruct the categorical syllogism used to attain an unknown conclusion.

Core Idea
Reflection is not random thought; it is a disciplined arrangement of known premises to reach a new unknown.

In this passage, the author defines nazar as a deliberate arrangement of known matters to achieve an unknown. This is not mere thinking; it is the foundational process of all theoretical inquiry in logic, whether you are defining a concept like 'angel' or proving a truth like 'the world is temporally originated'. The passage provides both a conceptual example and a complete categorical syllogism, showing that nazar underlies both tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent).

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of nazar
Find the formal definition: 'نظر عبارة عن ترتيب أمور معلومة ليتأدى ذلك الترتيب إلى تحصيل المجهول'. Identify the key components: arrangement (tartib), known matters (umur ma'luma), and attainment of the unknown (tahsil al-majhul).
The definition includes both the activity (tartib) and its purpose (tahsil al-majhul).
2
Identify the two types of unknowns
The passage distinguishes between conceptual unknown (tasawwuri) and assential unknown (tasdiqi). The conceptual example is the conception of jinn and angels; the assential example is proving the world's temporality.
Nazar applies to both realms, not just assent.
3
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism
From the assential example: major premise 'كل متغير حادث' (Everything changing is temporally originated), minor premise 'العالم متغير' (The world is changing), conclusion 'العالم حادث' (The world is temporally originated). Identify the middle term 'متغير' (changing).
This is a first-figure Barbara syllogism, universally valid.
4
Analyze the two movements of the soul
The first movement (haraka ula) is surveying stored knowns to find suitable principles. The second movement (haraka thaniyya) arranges them into a productive order. Trace this process in the world-temporality example.
These psychological steps mirror the logical structure of the syllogism.

أي تغير العالم وحدوث المتغير - مناسبان؛ فهذه الحركة تسمّى بالحركة الأولى، ثم تنتقل منها بأن يرتبها ترتيبا مؤديا إلى المطلوب، مثلا رتبت أن العالم متغيّر وكل متغير حادث، وهذا الترتيب يسمّى بالحركة الثانية، فبعد هذين الحركتين حصل المطلوب أي العالم حادث. ثم اعلم أنه قد يتفق أن النفس تكون مستشعرا للمطلوب بوجه من الوجوه، ثم تنتقل إلى المبادئ دفعة بلا تحتم =
Theoretical matters: Definition of reflection. Benefit: When you have understood what we have mentioned, that theoretical matters absolutely, whether conceptual or assential, are dependent on reflection and thought, then you must know the meaning of reflection. I say: Reflection in their terminology is an arrangement of known matters, so that that arrangement leads to the attainment of the unknown when the known information is arranged. The arrangement (tartib) linguistically is placing each thing in its rank, and terminologically is making multiple things such that the name of one applies to them, and their relation to each other is one of precedence and posteriority. For example, the matters you know in the conception of jinn: that it is a subtle fiery body that takes various forms, and among them are male and female. And you say in the conception of angels: they are a subtle luminous body that takes forms and there is no male or female among them. From that results knowledge of the reality of jinn and angels. Similarly, for acquiring assent from known premises and for the world's change and origination, you may say: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated.' After this composition, the knowledge of an unknown proposition is obtained, namely 'The world is temporally originated.' The intention with these arranged known matters in conception is the definer (mu'arraf) which is also called by the commentator 'proof' (hujja) and 'syllogism' (qiyas) in assent, and the unknown matters are called 'definiendum' (mu'arraf) in conceptions and 'conclusion' (natija) in assents. So it is in the commentary. And the intention with 'matters' (umur) is that which is more than one, for the plurals occurring in the definitions of the art intend that. 'So that it leads' etc.: An objection was raised that it excludes definition by a single term, like definition by the differentia alone or the property alone. It was answered sometimes that definition by a single term is only with derivatives, which are composite because they include the essence and the attribute; and sometimes that definition by a single term is not as comprehensive as definition by a composite. And the sheikh said: definition by a single term is rare and deficient, i.e., little and incomplete. The unknown (majhul) intended is the sought (matlub), whether conceptual or assential, which is obtained by arrangement. The arranged matters are known and lead to the sought which is unknown, but it is necessary that the sought be known to the seeker in some way, otherwise it would be seeking the absolutely unknown, which is impossible; and it must also be unknown, otherwise it would be learning the known and obtaining what is already obtained, which is also impossible, as established in its place. 'When arranged' etc.: Know that when the seeker wants to obtain an unknown, his soul turns to the known matters stored with it. Whatever it sees suitable for its sought, it takes, and whatever it sees unsuitable, it leaves until it encompasses the principles (mabadi') of the sought. For example, if he wants to ascertain whether the world is characterized by temporality or not, the soul turns to the stored information, then examines among the information and finds that the world changes and the thing characterized by change is characterized by temporality. So both—i.e., change of the world and temporality of the changing—are suitable. This movement is called the first movement. Then it moves from them by arranging them in an order leading to the sought. For example, he arranged: the world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated. This arrangement is called the second movement. After these two movements, the sought is obtained, i.e., the world is temporally originated. Then know that it may happen that the soul is already aware of the sought in some way, then moves to the principles in one stroke without needing to undergo =
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage defines nazar (reflection) as the arrangement of known matters to attain the unknown. It is an expansion probe explaining a compressed definition. The author first states that theoretical matters require reflection and thought, so the meaning of nazar must be learned. He gives the formal definition: 'arrangement of known matters so that arrangement leads to attainment of the unknown.' Then he clarifies the term 'arrangement' (tartib) both linguistically and terminologically, and provides two examples. The first example is conceptual: to conceive jinn and angels, we arrange known attributes (e.g., jinn are subtle fiery, angels are luminous) to obtain a complete conception. The second example is assential (syllogistic): from the known premises 'the world is changing' and 'everything changing is temporally originated', we infer the unknown conclusion 'the world is temporally originated'. This illustrates the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). The passage then explains that the arranged known matters are called 'definer' (mu'arraf) or 'proof' (hujja) and 'syllogism' (qiyas) in assents, and the unknown is called 'definiendum' or 'conclusion'. There is a discussion of the objection that definition by a single term (like 'differentia alone') would be excluded; the answer is that such definitions are either derivatives (implicitly composite) or rare and deficient. The author then elaborates on the conditions for the sought (matlub): it must be known in some respect (to avoid seeking the absolutely unknown) and also unknown in some respect (to avoid obtaining the already known). Finally, a psychological analysis is given: the seeker's soul first surveys stored knowns (first movement) to find suitable principles, then arranges them (second movement) to reach the unknown. This is illustrated with the example of proving the world's temporality.

The Syllogism
beginner

Simple Conversion: Definition and Proof for Universal Negative
Simple conversion definition and proof for universal negative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion and apply the reductio proof to verify the conversion of a universal negative proposition.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality.

Simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi) is a fundamental operation in immediate inference. It allows us to rearrange a proposition without losing its truth value, provided certain rules are followed. In this lesson, we begin with the definition and the most straightforward case: the universal negative, which converts to itself. The proof uses reductio ad absurdum (khalf), a powerful logical technique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of simple conversion
Simple conversion is defined as swapping the first part (subject) and second part (predicate) of a proposition while preserving truth and quality. Note that this is a swap in wording, not in real essence (subject remains subject in reality).
The term 'simple' indicates a straight path, unlike conversion by contradiction.
2
Understand the rule for universal negative
A universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No human is a stone') converts to itself: 'No stone is a human'. The quantity and quality remain the same.
This is the only type that converts to a universal (all others convert to particular or not at all).
3
Work through the reductio proof
Assume the converse is false, so its contradictory ('Some stone is a human') is true. Combine this with the original premise: 'Some stone is a human' + 'No human is a stone'. Using a syllogism (Barbara? Actually it's a first-figure syllogism: Some stone is human; no human is stone → some stone is not stone). This yields a contradiction, proving the converse must be true.
The proof shows that denying the converse leads to an impossibility.
4
Recognize the distinction between wording and reality
The swap is in linguistic terms only. In reality, the subject (essence) and predicate (description) retain their roles. Conversion does not change the ontological status.
This clarification prevents metaphysical confusion.

العكس المستوي، ويقال له: العكس المستقيم أيضا، وهو عبارة عن جعل الجزء الأول من القضية ثانيا، والجزء الثاني أولا مع بقاء الصدق والكيف؛ فالسالبة الكلية تنعكس كنفسها كقولك: "لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر" ينعكس إلى قولك: "لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان" بدليل الخلف. تقريره: أنه لو لم يصدق "لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان" عند صدق قولنا: "لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر" لصدق نقيضه – أعني قولنا: "بعض الحجر إنسان" – فنضمه مع الأصل ونقول: "بعض الحجر إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر ينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر"؛ فيلزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه، وذلك محال. العكس المستوي إلخ: اعلم أن العكس يطلق على المعنى المصدري أي تبديل طرفي القضية، وعلى العادة بتقديم عكس السوالب؛ لأن منها ما ينعكس كلية، والكلي وإن كان سلبا أشرف من الجزئي وإن كان إيجابا؛ لأنه أفيد في العلوم وأضبط. وذلك محال إلخ: اعلم أن للقوم في بيان عكوس القضايا ثلاث طرق: الخلف: وهو ضم نقيض العكس لينتج محالا، كما عرفت في المثال المذكور في المتن. 2- والافتراض: وهو فرض ذات الموضوع شيئا معينا، وحمل وصفي الموضوع والمحمول عليه ليحصل مفهوم العكس، وهو لا يجري إلا في الموجبات والسوالب المركبة بخلاف الخلف؛ فإنه يعم الجميع. 3- والثالث طريق العكس: وهو أن يعكس نقيض العكس ليحصل ما ينافي الأصل. والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس لزوما؛ الجواز عموم الموضوع في الحملية والمقدم في الشرطية، مثلا يصدق أعني المستقبل - فافهم.
Simple conversion, also called direct conversion, is making the first part of the proposition second and the second part first while preserving truth and quality. The universal negative converts to itself, as in your saying: 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human', by proof of reductio. Its exposition: If 'No stone is a human' were not true when 'No human is a stone' is true, then its contradictory – namely 'Some stone is a human' – would be true. Then we join it with the original and say: 'Some stone is a human, and no human is a stone, yields some stone is not a stone'; thus negation of a thing from itself follows, and that is impossible. 'Simple conversion etc.': Know that 'conversion' is used for the verbal noun meaning exchanging the two terms of a proposition, and for the proposition resulting from the exchange; the author conducted the discussion according to the first usage. It is described as 'simple' because it is a straight path without crookedness, unlike conversion by contradiction, which is not a clear path. 'Making the first part etc.': What is meant by the first and second parts are the parts in mention, not in reality; for the first and second parts in reality are the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate, and conversion does not make the essence of the subject a predicate nor the description of the predicate a subject. 'The universal negative etc.': The custom is to present the conversion of negatives first, because among them is what converts as universal, and the universal even if negative is nobler than the particular even if affirmative, because it is more beneficial in the sciences and more precise. 'That is impossible etc.': Know that the scholars have three methods in explaining the conversions of propositions: 1) Reductio: joining the contradictory of the converse to produce an impossibility, as you learned in the example mentioned in the text. 2) Supposition: supposing the essence of the subject as a specific thing and attributing the two descriptions of subject and predicate to it to obtain the meaning of the converse; it only applies to affirmatives and compound negatives, unlike reductio, which covers all. 3) The third is the method of conversion: converting the contradictory of the converse to obtain something that contradicts the original. The particular negative does not convert necessarily; permissibility of the subject's generality in categorical [propositions] and the antecedent in conditional ones; for example, 'Some animal is not a human' is true but 'Some human is not an animal' is not true. The universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative; our saying 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human', and does not convert to a universal affirmative, because the predicate or consequent may be general, as in our example, so 'Every animal is a human' is not true. Here is a doubt: its exposition is that our saying 'Every old man was once young' is a true universal affirmative, yet its converse 'Some young person was once old' is not true. It was answered: its converse is not what you mentioned, but rather its converse is 'Some of those who were young is old.' But 'its converse...etc.' has apparent objection; for it is not a particular negative, and 'was' is a copula, and due to its lack of independence it is not suitable as a predicate nor as part of the predicate; so the predicate is only 'young', and in the converse it must be subject. And it may be answered by another face: that the original is an absolute temporal proposition and it does not convert as absolute; so the correct thing is to say: This proposition judges the affirmation of the predicate as a temporary affirmation in the past tense. So it is an absolute temporal if necessity is not considered, and an absolute temporal if considered, and both convert to a general absolute. So its converse is 'Some young person is old' actually, and it is necessarily true; because some of what is true of young in one time – namely the past – is old in one time – namely the future. So understand.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the topic of simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi). It begins by defining simple conversion: swapping the two parts (subject and predicate) of a proposition while keeping truth and quality. The first rule discussed is the universal negative (saliba kulliya). The commentator proves that a universal negative converts to itself (e.g., 'No human is a stone' becomes 'No stone is a human'). The proof is by reductio (khalf): assume the converse is false, then its contradictory ('Some stone is a human') is true. Combining this with the original premise yields 'Some stone is not a stone', a contradiction. The commentator then notes that 'conversion' can refer to the act or the resulting proposition; the author uses the first sense. The term 'simple' indicates a straightforward path, unlike conversion by contradiction. A clarification is added: swapping parts is in wording, not reality—the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate remain distinct. The commentator explains why negative conversions are presented first: some (the universal negative) convert as universal, and universal statements (even negative) are more beneficial in the sciences than particular ones. Three methods for proving conversions are listed: (1) reductio (khalf), (2) supposition (iftirad), and (3) conversion of the contradictory (aks al-naqid). Next, the particular negative is examined: it does not convert necessarily. An example shows that 'Some animal is not a human' is true, but its converse 'Some human is not an animal' is false. Then the universal affirmative: it converts to a particular affirmative, not to a universal affirmative, because the predicate may be broader. For instance, 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human', not 'Every animal is a human'. A doubt is raised regarding 'Every old man was once young': a universal affirmative whose apparent converse 'Some young person was once old' is false. The first answer suggests the correct converse is 'Some of those who were young is old'. The commentator notes an objection: 'was' is a copula, not part of the predicate, so it cannot reappear as subject. A second answer is preferred: the original is a temporal absolute proposition (mutlaqa waqtiyya), which converts to a general absolute. Thus the true converse is 'Some young person is old' in actuality, because some individuals who were young in the past become old in the future. The commentator ends with 'so understand', emphasizing the need for careful temporal analysis.

The Syllogism

Rules for Particular Negative and Universal Affirmative Conversion
Rules for particular negative and universal affirmative conversion, with doubt resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct converse of a particular negative and a universal affirmative proposition, and handle temporal exceptions.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative; a particular negative does not convert necessarily.

Not all propositions convert in the same way. While universal negatives convert nicely, universal affirmatives only convert to particulars, and particular negatives do not convert at all. Moreover, temporal propositions require careful analysis to avoid errors. This lesson examines these rules and resolves a classic doubt about the conversion of 'Every old man was once young.'

Guided Path
1
State the rule for particular negative
A particular negative (e.g., 'Some animal is not a human') does not convert necessarily. Its converse 'Some human is not an animal' is false because the predicate may be broader.
The reason is that the subject may be more general than the predicate in the original.
2
State the rule for universal affirmative
A universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') converts to a particular affirmative ('Some animal is a human'), not to a universal affirmative. The predicate may be universal.
If the predicate is broader, the universal converse would be false.
3
Examine the doubt about temporal propositions
The proposition 'Every old man was once young' seems to be a universal affirmative, but its apparent converse 'Some young person was once old' is false. This is a puzzle.
The doubt arises because 'was' indicates a past tense copula.
4
Resolve the doubt using temporal analysis
The original is not a simple universal affirmative but a temporal absolute (mutlaqa waqtiyya). Its correct converse is 'Some young person is old' (in actuality), because the judgment is about time: those who were young in the past become old in the future.
The copula 'was' cannot be repeated as subject; the predicate in the converse must be adjusted to reflect the temporal aspect.

المركبة بخلاف الخلف؛ فإنه يعم الجميع. 3- والثالث طريق العكس: وهو أن يعكس نقيض العكس ليحصل ما ينافي الأصل. والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس لزوما؛ الجواز عموم الموضوع في الحملية والمقدم في الشرطية، مثلا يصدق بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان وليس يصدق "بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان". والموجبة الكلية تنعكس إلى موجبة جزئية، فقولنا: "كل إنسان حيوان" ينعكس إلى قولنا "بعض الحيوان إنسان"، ولا ينعكس إلى موجبة كلية؛ لأنه يجوز أن يكون المحمول أو التالي عاما، كما في مثالنا فلا يصدق "كل حيوان إنسان". وههنا شك تقريره أن قولنا: "كل شيخ كان شابا" موجبة كلية صادقة مع أن عكسه "بعض الشاب كان شيخا" ليس بصادق؟ وأجيب عنه: بأن عكسه ليس ما ذكرت، بل عكسه "بعض من كان شابا شيخ". بل عكسه "بعض إلخ: فيه نظر ظاهر؛ فإن غير سالبة جزئية، وهي "كان" رابطة، وهي لعدم استقلالها لا تصلح للمحمولية ولا لوقوعها جزءا من المحمول، فالمحمول هو الشاب فقط، ففي العكس لا بد أن يكون موضوعا. وقد يجاب بوجه آخر، وهو أن الأصل مطلقة وقتية وهي لا تنعكس أي مطلقة فالصواب أن يقال: إن هذه القضية حكم فيها بثبوت المحمول ثبوتا موقتا بزمان الماضي، فهي مطلقة وقتية إن لم أعني المستقبل - فافهم.
Simple conversion, also called direct conversion, is making the first part of the proposition second and the second part first while preserving truth and quality. The universal negative converts to itself, as in your saying: 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human', by proof of reductio. Its exposition: If 'No stone is a human' were not true when 'No human is a stone' is true, then its contradictory – namely 'Some stone is a human' – would be true. Then we join it with the original and say: 'Some stone is a human, and no human is a stone, yields some stone is not a stone'; thus negation of a thing from itself follows, and that is impossible. 'Simple conversion etc.': Know that 'conversion' is used for the verbal noun meaning exchanging the two terms of a proposition, and for the proposition resulting from the exchange; the author conducted the discussion according to the first usage. It is described as 'simple' because it is a straight path without crookedness, unlike conversion by contradiction, which is not a clear path. 'Making the first part etc.': What is meant by the first and second parts are the parts in mention, not in reality; for the first and second parts in reality are the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate, and conversion does not make the essence of the subject a predicate nor the description of the predicate a subject. 'The universal negative etc.': The custom is to present the conversion of negatives first, because among them is what converts as universal, and the universal even if negative is nobler than the particular even if affirmative, because it is more beneficial in the sciences and more precise. 'That is impossible etc.': Know that the scholars have three methods in explaining the conversions of propositions: 1) Reductio: joining the contradictory of the converse to produce an impossibility, as you learned in the example mentioned in the text. 2) Supposition: supposing the essence of the subject as a specific thing and attributing the two descriptions of subject and predicate to it to obtain the meaning of the converse; it only applies to affirmatives and compound negatives, unlike reductio, which covers all. 3) The third is the method of conversion: converting the contradictory of the converse to obtain something that contradicts the original. The particular negative does not convert necessarily; permissibility of the subject's generality in categorical [propositions] and the antecedent in conditional ones; for example, 'Some animal is not a human' is true but 'Some human is not an animal' is not true. The universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative; our saying 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human', and does not convert to a universal affirmative, because the predicate or consequent may be general, as in our example, so 'Every animal is a human' is not true. Here is a doubt: its exposition is that our saying 'Every old man was once young' is a true universal affirmative, yet its converse 'Some young person was once old' is not true. It was answered: its converse is not what you mentioned, but rather its converse is 'Some of those who were young is old.' But 'its converse...etc.' has apparent objection; for it is not a particular negative, and 'was' is a copula, and due to its lack of independence it is not suitable as a predicate nor as part of the predicate; so the predicate is only 'young', and in the converse it must be subject. And it may be answered by another face: that the original is an absolute temporal proposition and it does not convert as absolute; so the correct thing is to say: This proposition judges the affirmation of the predicate as a temporary affirmation in the past tense. So it is an absolute temporal if necessity is not considered, and an absolute temporal if considered, and both convert to a general absolute. So its converse is 'Some young person is old' actually, and it is necessarily true; because some of what is true of young in one time – namely the past – is old in one time – namely the future. So understand.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the topic of simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi). It begins by defining simple conversion: swapping the two parts (subject and predicate) of a proposition while keeping truth and quality. The first rule discussed is the universal negative (saliba kulliya). The commentator proves that a universal negative converts to itself (e.g., 'No human is a stone' becomes 'No stone is a human'). The proof is by reductio (khalf): assume the converse is false, then its contradictory ('Some stone is a human') is true. Combining this with the original premise yields 'Some stone is not a stone', a contradiction. The commentator then notes that 'conversion' can refer to the act or the resulting proposition; the author uses the first sense. The term 'simple' indicates a straightforward path, unlike conversion by contradiction. A clarification is added: swapping parts is in wording, not reality—the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate remain distinct. The commentator explains why negative conversions are presented first: some (the universal negative) convert as universal, and universal statements (even negative) are more beneficial in the sciences than particular ones. Three methods for proving conversions are listed: (1) reductio (khalf), (2) supposition (iftirad), and (3) conversion of the contradictory (aks al-naqid). Next, the particular negative is examined: it does not convert necessarily. An example shows that 'Some animal is not a human' is true, but its converse 'Some human is not an animal' is false. Then the universal affirmative: it converts to a particular affirmative, not to a universal affirmative, because the predicate may be broader. For instance, 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human', not 'Every animal is a human'. A doubt is raised regarding 'Every old man was once young': a universal affirmative whose apparent converse 'Some young person was once old' is false. The first answer suggests the correct converse is 'Some of those who were young is old'. The commentator notes an objection: 'was' is a copula, not part of the predicate, so it cannot reappear as subject. A second answer is preferred: the original is a temporal absolute proposition (mutlaqa waqtiyya), which converts to a general absolute. Thus the true converse is 'Some young person is old' in actuality, because some individuals who were young in the past become old in the future. The commentator ends with 'so understand', emphasizing the need for careful temporal analysis.

المطلقة الوقتية

The Three Divisions of Argument and the Definition of Syllogism
Introduces the three divisions of hujja and defines qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of argument and explain the definition of qiyas as a composite statement, distinguishing its rational and verbal forms.

Core Idea
Argument is either syllogism, induction, or analogy; syllogism is a composite statement, existing both rationally and verbally.

Now that we have studied propositions and their inversions, we turn to the heart of logic: argument (hujja). The author presents three divisions of argument: syllogism, induction, and analogy. In this lesson, we will focus on the first division—syllogism—and unpack its definition as a composite statement, noting the important distinction between rational and verbal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three divisions of argument
In the Arabic passage, find the three terms: القياس (al-qiyas), الاستقراء (al-istiqrāʼ), and التمثيل (al-tamthīl). They are the three parts of hujja.
2
Identify the definition of qiyas
The passage defines qiyas as 'قول مؤلّف' (qawl muʼallaf), a composite statement. 'Composite' means it is made up of premises. This definition distinguishes qiyas from simple statements.
3
Distinguish between verbal and rational syllogism
The commentary explains that 'qawl' can be verbal (لفظي) or intellectual (عقلي). Similarly, qiyas has audible (مسموع) and rational (معقول) forms. Note which form is needed for demonstration (rational) and which for dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, or sophistical arguments (verbal).
4
Understand the purpose of each type
Demonstration (برهان) aims at objective truth regardless of audience, so rational syllogism suffices. Other arguments engage others for social order, so they require verbal expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وإذ قد فرغنا عن مباحث القضايا والعكوس التي كانت من مبادئ الحجة، فحري بنا أن نتكلم في مباحث الحجة، فنقول: الحجة على ثلاثة أقسام: أحدها: القياس وثانيها الاستقراء وثالثها التمثيل فلنبين هذه الثلاثة في ثلاثة فصول. فصل في القياس: وهو قول مؤلّف مع شرح: اعلم أن القول يطلق بالاشتراك على الملفوظ وعلى المفهوم العقلي، كما أن القياس يطلق بالاشتراك والتشابه على القياس المسموع والقياس المعقول؛ فالملفوظ جنس للقياس المسموع والمفهوم العقلي للمعقول. وإنما يكتفى بالقياس المعقول وحده إذا كان المطلوب برهانيا. وأما إذا كان جدليا، أو خطابيا، أو شعريا، أو مغالطيا فهو محتاج إلى القياس الملفوظ؛ لأن منفعة ما سوى البرهان بحسب الغير ولمصلحة التمدن، وأما البرهان فلتحصيل ما عليه الحق في نفسه، ولا مدخل للغير والاجتماع فيه. وذكر "المؤلف" بعد القول إما =
Chapter: Since we have finished the discussions of propositions and their inversions, which were among the principles of the argument, it is fitting for us to speak about the discussions of the argument. So we say: The argument is of three divisions: One: the syllogism, second: induction, third: analogy. So let us explain these three in three chapters. Chapter on the syllogism: It is a composite statement. Commentary: Know that 'statement' (qawl) is used homonymously for the verbal utterance and the intellectual concept, just as 'syllogism' (qiyas) is used homonymously and by resemblance for the audible syllogism and the rational syllogism. So the verbal utterance is the genus for the audible syllogism, and the intellectual concept for the rational. One is satisfied with the rational syllogism alone when the required [knowledge] is demonstrative. But if it is dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, or sophistical, then it needs the verbal syllogism; because the benefit of what is other than demonstration is with respect to others and for the purpose of social order. As for demonstration, it is for attaining what is true in itself, and there is no role for others and society in it. And the author's mentioning 'composite' after 'statement' is either...
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage marks a transition from the study of propositions and their inversions to the study of argument (hujja), which is the central topic of logic. The author states that argument has three types: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). He will dedicate a separate chapter to each. The focus here is on qiyas, defined as 'a composite statement' (qawl mu'allaf). The commentary expands on this definition by clarifying that 'statement' (qawl) can refer either to a verbal utterance (lafz) or to an intellectual concept (ma'qul). Similarly, 'syllogism' can be either audible (masmu') or rational (ma'qul). The verbal utterance is the genus for the audible syllogism, and the intellectual concept for the rational syllogism. The rational syllogism alone suffices for demonstrative proofs (burhan), which aim at objective truth irrespective of audience. However, for dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, or sophistical arguments—which engage others and serve social purposes—the verbal syllogism (expressed in language) is necessary. The commentator notes that the author's insertion of 'composite' after 'statement' is for a reason, but the text cuts off before explaining further.

The Syllogism

Definition of Qiyas (Syllogism)
Author defines qiyas as a composed argument that entails a new conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the definition of qiyas (syllogism) from its logical components and distinguish it from other types of proof.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a composed argument that forces a new truth from granted premises.

Before diving into the types of syllogism, we must first grasp what makes an argument a qiyas. This foundational definition sets the stage for all logical reasoning in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of qiyas
The author places qiyas as one of three types of proof (hujja). Note the others: istiqra' (induction) and tamthil (analogy).
2
Parse the definition
Break down 'qawl mu'allaf min qadaya': a composed statement from propositions. What does 'composed' mean here?
3
Understand the consequence
The key phrase: 'yalzim minha qawl akhar' – another statement necessarily follows. This is the deductive necessity of the syllogism.
4
Note the condition of taslim
The conclusion only follows if the premises are granted (taslim). This distinguishes demonstration from persuasion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وإذ قد فرغنا عن مباحث القضايا والعكوس التي كانت من مبادئ الحجة، فحري بنا أن نتكلم في مباحث الحجة، فنقول: الحجة على ثلاثة أقسام: أحدها: القياس وثانيها الاستقراء وثالثها التمثيل فلنبين هذه الثلاثة في ثلاثة فصول. فصل في القياس: وهو قول مؤلّف من قضايا يلزم عنها قول آخر بعد تسليم تلك القضايا. فإن كان النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورا فيه يسمّى استثنائيا، كقولنا: "إن كان زيد إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه إنسان" ينتج فهو حيوان. وإن كان زيد حمارا كان ناهقا لكنه ليس بناهق ينتج ليس بحمار. وإن لم تكن النتيجة ونقيضها مذكورا يسمّى اقترانيا كقولك: "زيد إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان" ينتج "زيد حيوان".
Section: Since we have finished discussing propositions and their conversions, which were among the principles of proof, it is fitting for us to speak about the topics of proof. We say: Proof is of three types: one is qiyas, the second is istiqra', and the third is tamthil. We will explain these three in three chapters. Section on qiyas: It is a composed statement from propositions from which another statement necessarily follows, given those propositions are granted. If the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it, it is called istithna'i, such as our saying: 'If Zayd is a human, then he is an animal; but he is a human' yields 'so he is an animal.' And if Zayd is a donkey, then he is a brayer; but he is not a brayer' yields 'he is not a donkey.' And if the conclusion and its contradictory are not mentioned, it is called iqtirani, such as your saying: 'Zayd is a human, and every human is an animal' yields 'Zayd is an animal.'
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage. The author first situates the discussion of proof (hujja) after having completed the treatment of propositions and their conversions. He states that proof is of three types: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). Then he focuses on qiyas, which is the primary tool for certain knowledge. The definition given is: qiyas is a composed statement (qawl mu'allaf) from propositions (qadaya) from which another statement (qawl akhar) necessarily follows (yalzim) upon the granting of those propositions. The author then divides qiyas into two types based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned within the syllogism. If the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned, it is called istithna'i (exceptive syllogism). Two examples are given from conditional reasoning: one with an affirmative consequent (If Zayd is human, he is an animal; he is human, therefore he is an animal) and one with a negative consequent (If Zayd is a donkey, he brays; he does not bray, therefore he is not a donkey). If neither the conclusion nor its contradictory is mentioned, it is called iqtirani (conjunctive/categorical syllogism), exemplified by the simple categorical argument: Zayd is a human; every human is an animal; therefore Zayd is an animal. This classification is foundational: the iqtirani corresponds to Aristotle's categorical syllogism, while the istithna'i corresponds to the conditional or disjunctive syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس
الحجة
الاستقراء
التمثيل
قول مؤلّف
تسليم

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is the foundational logical tool for deriving certain conclusions from granted premises in Islamic sciences.

The Two Types of Qiyas: Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author divides qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani based on mention of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any syllogism as iqtirani or istithna'i based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned.

Core Idea
The presence or absence of the conclusion in the premises determines the type of syllogism.

Once we have defined qiyas, the author immediately divides it into two categories: iqtirani and istithna'i. This distinction is crucial for understanding how different syllogistic forms work and how to analyze arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of qiyas
Qiyas is a composed argument leading to a conclusion.
2
Identify the criterion for division
The author uses whether the conclusion (natija) or its contradictory (naqid) is explicitly mentioned within the syllogism.
3
Understand istithna'i
If the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned, it is istithna'i. Example: 'If Zayd is human, then he is animal; but he is human' – the conclusion 'he is animal' is mentioned.
4
Understand iqtirani
If neither the conclusion nor its contradictory is mentioned, it is iqtirani. Example: 'Zayd is human, and every human is animal' – the conclusion 'Zayd is animal' is not mentioned explicitly.
5
Test the classification
Given a syllogism, decide which type it is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وإذ قد فرغنا عن مباحث القضايا والعكوس التي كانت من مبادئ الحجة، فحري بنا أن نتكلم في مباحث الحجة، فنقول: الحجة على ثلاثة أقسام: أحدها: القياس وثانيها الاستقراء وثالثها التمثيل فلنبين هذه الثلاثة في ثلاثة فصول. فصل في القياس: وهو قول مؤلّف من قضايا يلزم عنها قول آخر بعد تسليم تلك القضايا. فإن كان النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورا فيه يسمّى استثنائيا، كقولنا: "إن كان زيد إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه إنسان" ينتج فهو حيوان. وإن كان زيد حمارا كان ناهقا لكنه ليس بناهق ينتج ليس بحمار. وإن لم تكن النتيجة ونقيضها مذكورا يسمّى اقترانيا كقولك: "زيد إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان" ينتج "زيد حيوان".
Section: Since we have finished discussing propositions and their conversions, which were among the principles of proof, it is fitting for us to speak about the topics of proof. We say: Proof is of three types: one is qiyas, the second is istiqra', and the third is tamthil. We will explain these three in three chapters. Section on qiyas: It is a composed statement from propositions from which another statement necessarily follows, given those propositions are granted. If the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it, it is called istithna'i, such as our saying: 'If Zayd is a human, then he is an animal; but he is a human' yields 'so he is an animal.' And if Zayd is a donkey, then he is a brayer; but he is not a brayer' yields 'he is not a donkey.' And if the conclusion and its contradictory are not mentioned, it is called iqtirani, such as your saying: 'Zayd is a human, and every human is an animal' yields 'Zayd is an animal.'
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage. The author first situates the discussion of proof (hujja) after having completed the treatment of propositions and their conversions. He states that proof is of three types: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). Then he focuses on qiyas, which is the primary tool for certain knowledge. The definition given is: qiyas is a composed statement (qawl mu'allaf) from propositions (qadaya) from which another statement (qawl akhar) necessarily follows (yalzim) upon the granting of those propositions. The author then divides qiyas into two types based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned within the syllogism. If the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned, it is called istithna'i (exceptive syllogism). Two examples are given from conditional reasoning: one with an affirmative consequent (If Zayd is human, he is an animal; he is human, therefore he is an animal) and one with a negative consequent (If Zayd is a donkey, he brays; he does not bray, therefore he is not a donkey). If neither the conclusion nor its contradictory is mentioned, it is called iqtirani (conjunctive/categorical syllogism), exemplified by the simple categorical argument: Zayd is a human; every human is an animal; therefore Zayd is an animal. This classification is foundational: the iqtirani corresponds to Aristotle's categorical syllogism, while the istithna'i corresponds to the conditional or disjunctive syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
النتيجة
النقيض

C) It must contain a conditional premise.
D) The premises must be self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The classification into iqtirani and istithna'i helps us quickly identify the logical form of a syllogism in Islamic arguments.

Qiyas Iqtirani: Categorical and Conditional Syllogisms
Author defines qiyas iqtirani and divides it into hamli and sharti types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hamli and sharti types of qiyas iqtirani and identify the key structural features of each.

Core Idea
Qiyas iqtirani splits into categorical and conditional types; the conditional may mix with categoricals.

This lesson unpacks the foundational division of the qiyas iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism) into its two main types: hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional). The author and commentators clarify the definitions, addressing nuances like intrinsic necessity and the naming of mixed syllogisms. Understanding this division is critical for recognizing the basic building blocks of logical inference in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Locate where the author states that qiyas iqtirani is divided into hamli and sharti. These are the two fundamental categories.
2
Examine the definition of hamli
Hamli is composed of two categorical propositions. Note that it includes three terms: subject, predicate, and the repeated middle term.
3
Examine the definition of sharti
Sharti is not composed of two categoricals; it may be two conditionals or a conditional and a categorical. Understand why the mixed type is called 'conditional' by naming after the predominant part.
4
Note the condition for 'luzum'
The commentary emphasizes that necessity (luzum) must be intrinsic, excluding external premises as in qiyas al-musawat. This ensures the syllogism's self-sufficiency.
5
Apply the classification
Practice classifying sample syllogisms as hamli or sharti to reinforce the conceptual distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاقتراني وهو :قسمان حملي وشرطي وموضوع النتيجة في القياس مستدرك كما قال شارح المطالع "، أو احتراز عن كون "من" تبعيضية كما صرح به السيد المحقق في "شرح المواقف"، أو أورد ليصح تعلق "من" به، كما صرح العلامة التفتازاني. من قضايا إلخ: المراد بـ "القضايا" ما فوق الواحد، فلا يكون القضية الواحدة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها قياسا يلزم إلخ ينبغي أن يراد باللزوم في قوله: "يلزم" اللزوم الذاتي، كما هو مصرّح في التعريف المشهور هنا: قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلّمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر ؛ فيخرج ما يستلزم قولا آخر لذاته، بل بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية كما في قياس المساوات، وهو ما يتركب من قضيتين متعلق محمول إحداهما يكون موضوعا لأخرى كقولنا: أ مساوٍ لـ ب، وب مساوٍ لـ ج؛ فهما يستلزمان أن أ مساو لـ ج، لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة أن مساوي المساوي مساو، حتى لو لم يتحقق لم ينتج شيئا. ثم اعلم أن المراد باللزوم في قوله: "يلزم" أعم من اللازم البين وغير البين؛ ليندرج فيه القياس الكامل، وهو الشكل الأول، وغير الكامل وهو باقي الأشكال. بعد تسليم إلخ إشارة إلى أن مقدمات القياس لا تحب أن تكون مسلّمة في أنفسها، بل إنها وإن كانت كاذبة منكرة لكن هي بحيث لو سُلّمت لزم عنها قول آخر؛ فيدخل في التعريف القياس الصادق المقدمات وغيره كقولنا الإنسان حجر وكل حجر جماد؛ فإن هاتين القضيتين وإن كانتا كاذبتين إلا أنهما بحيث لو سلّمتها لزم عنها أن كل إنسان جماد فصل في القياس الاقتراني إلخ لما فرغ من تعريف القياس وتقسيمه إلى قسمين شرع في الأقسام وابتدأ بالاقتراني المركب من الحمليات وهو يشتمل على حدود ثلاثة: - ١ موضوع المطلوب. ٢- ومحموله. ٣- والمكرر بينهما في المقدمتين؛ فقال: "فصل في القياس الاقتراني". شرطي القياس الشرطي ما لا يكون مركبا من حمليتين سواء كان مركبا من شرطيتين، أو من شرطية وحملية؛ فتسمية الأول بالشرطي ظاهر وأما تسمية المركب من الشرطية والحملية فتسمية الكل باسم الجزء الأعظم.
Chapter on the conjunctive syllogism: It is of two types: categorical and conditional. ... (commentary on definition details) ... The conjunctive syllogism composed of categoricals includes three terms: subject of the desired conclusion, its predicate, and the repeated term between them in the two premises. ... Conditional: The conditional syllogism is what is not composed of two categoricals, whether it is composed of two conditionals or of a conditional and a categorical; thus naming the first as 'conditional' is apparent, and as for naming the composite of conditional and categorical, it is naming the whole after the major part.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines and expands upon the qiyas iqtirani (conjunctive or categorical syllogism). The author begins by stating that qiyas iqtirani has two types: hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional). The commentary then delves into nuances of the definition of qiyas, discussing that 'luzum' (necessity) should be intrinsic, excluding cases like qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) which requires an external premise. It also clarifies that the premises need not be true in themselves; they only need to be such that if granted, the conclusion follows—as in the example 'man is stone, and every stone is mineral' yields 'every man is mineral,' even though false. Then returning to the division, the author notes that the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) is composed of three terms: the subject of the desired conclusion, its predicate, and the middle term repeated in the two premises. The conditional syllogism (sharti) is defined as that which is not composed of two categorical propositions; it can be composed of two conditionals or of one conditional and one categorical. The commentator explains that naming the latter as 'conditional' is by way of naming the whole after its predominant part. This passage is essential for understanding the structure of syllogisms in logic.

Key Takeaway
The qiyas iqtirani divides into hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional); the sharti can be composed of two conditionals or a conditional and a categorical, named after the predominant part.

Why a Single Proposition Is Not a Syllogism
Clarifies that 'qadaya' means more than one proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a single proposition entailing its converse does not meet the definition of a syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism requires at least two premises.

In the definition of qiyas, the term 'qadaya' (propositions) appears, but what exactly does it mean? In this lesson, we examine the commentator's clarification that 'qadaya' must refer to more than one proposition. This seemingly simple point excludes certain arguments from being considered syllogisms, such as a single premise that entails its own converse. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly applying the definition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of qiyas
Remember that qiyas is defined as a statement composed of statements that, when accepted, entail by themselves another statement. The word 'statements' (qadaya) is key.
2
Understand the issue
The commentator explains that 'qadaya' must mean more than one. Ask: why would someone think a single proposition could be a syllogism? Consider that a proposition like 'Every A is B' entails its converse 'Some B is A' (by conversion). Is that a syllogism?
3
Apply the condition
Since the definition requires multiple propositions, a single proposition entailing its converse does not count because there is only one original proposition. Therefore, it is not a qiyas.
This seems obvious, but it clarifies that qiyas is essentially a compound argument, not a single statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستدرك كما قال شارح المطالع، أو احتراز عن كون "من" تبعيضية كما صرح به السيد المحقق في "شرح المواقف"، أو أورد ليصح تعلق "من" به، كما صرح العلامة التفتازاني. من قضايا إلخ: المراد بـ "القضايا" ما فوق الواحد، فلا يكون القضية الواحدة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها قياسا يلزم إلخ ينبغي أن يراد باللزوم في قوله: "يلزم" اللزوم الذاتي، كما هو مصرّح في التعريف المشهور هنا: قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلّمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر؛ فيخرج ما يستلزم قولا آخر لذاته، بل بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية كما في قياس المساوات، وهو ما يتركب من قضيتين متعلق محمول إحداهما يكون موضوعا لأخرى كقولنا: أ مساوٍ لـ ب، وب مساوٍ لـ ج؛ فهما يستلزمان أن أ مساو لـ ج، لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة أن مساوي المساوي مساو، حتى لو لم يتحقق لم ينتج شيئا. ثم اعلم أن المراد باللزوم في قوله: "يلزم" أعم من اللازم البين وغير البين؛ ليندرج فيه القياس الكامل، وهو الشكل الأول، وغير الكامل وهو باقي الأشكال. بعد تسليم إلخ إشارة إلى أن مقدمات القياس لا تحب أن تكون مسلّمة في أنفسها، بل إنها وإن كانت كاذبة منكرة لكن هي بحيث لو سُلّمت لزم عنها قول آخر؛ فيدخل في التعريف القياس الصادق المقدمات وغيره كقولنا الإنسان حجر وكل حجر جماد؛ فإن هاتين القضيتين وإن كانتا كاذبتين إلا أنهما بحيث لو سلّمتها لزم عنها أن كل إنسان جماد
Additional consideration: as the commentator of al-Matali' said, or as a precaution against 'min' being partitive as expressed by al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq in Sharh al-Mawaqif, or introduced to validate the attachment of 'min' to it, as expressed by al-Allama al-Taftazani. 'From propositions etc.': what is meant by 'propositions' is more than one, so a single proposition entailing its converse or the converse of its contrary is not a syllogism. 'Entails etc.': it should be understood by 'entails' in his statement the essential entailment, as is declared in the famous definition here: 'A statement composed of statements which, when accepted, entail by themselves another statement'; so it excludes what entails another statement not by itself, but through an extraneous premise, as in the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions such that the predicate of one is the subject of the other, like our statement: 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C'; these two entail that A is equal to C, but not by themselves, rather through the fact that the equal of the equal is equal, such that if it were not established, nothing would be produced. Then know that by 'entails' in his statement is meant the entailment that is broader than the obvious and non-obvious, so that it includes the perfect syllogism, which is the first figure, and the imperfect, which are the remaining figures. 'After acceptance etc.' is an indication that the premises of the syllogism do not have to be accepted in themselves; rather, even if they are false and denied, they are such that if they were accepted, they would entail another statement; thus the definition includes the syllogism with true premises and others, like our statement: 'Man is a stone, and every stone is inanimate'; these two propositions, though false, are such that if you accept them, they entail that every man is inanimate.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the previous definition of qiyas. It clarifies three key points. First, the word 'qadaya' (propositions) must refer to more than one, so a single proposition that implies its converse is not a syllogism. Second, the entailment (luzum) in the definition must be essential (dhati), not through an external premise. The example given is the 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat): 'A equals B, B equals C' only entails 'A equals C' via the axiom that equals of equals are equal. Without that axiom, the entailment fails, so it is not a bona fide syllogism as per the strict definition. Third, the entailment can be obvious or non-obvious, so both perfect (first figure) and imperfect (other figures) syllogisms are included. Additionally, the premises do not need to be true; even false premises can form a syllogism as long as they hypothetically entail a conclusion. The example of 'Man is a stone' and 'Every stone is inanimate' shows that a valid syllogism in form can have false premises.

Key Terms
قضايا
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it is not a valid inference.
B) Because the definition requires more than one proposition.
C) Because the entailment is not essential.
D) Because the proposition is false.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism must consist of at least two propositions; a single proposition, even if it entails something, is insufficient.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Essential Entailment: The Heart of the Syllogism
Explains essential entailment and excludes qiyas al-musawat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential entailment from entailment via an extraneous premise, using the example of the syllogism of equality.

Core Idea
A true syllogism requires that the conclusion follows from the premises alone, without needing an external axiom.

The commentary emphasizes that the entailment in the definition must be essential (luzum dhati). This means the conclusion must follow from the premises themselves, not through an extra premise that is not part of the argument. The famous counterexample is the 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat), which requires the axiom that equals of equals are equal. This lesson unpacks why such arguments are excluded and what that teaches us about the structure of syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'essential entailment'
The phrase 'by themselves' in the definition means that the conclusion is entailed solely by the premises without any additional assumption. This is called luzum dhati.
2
Examine the example of equality
Look at the argument: A equals B, B equals C, therefore A equals C. Does the conclusion follow from the two premises alone? No, because we need to know that 'equals of equals are equal' – that is an external axiom not stated.
3
Apply the condition
If the argument requires an unstated premise to be valid, it does not count as a syllogism in the strict sense. So the syllogism of equality is excluded.
4
Generalize
Any argument that depends on a principle not explicitly included in the premises is not a qiyas according to this definition. This ensures that syllogisms are self-contained proofs.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

B) Because the entailment is not essential but requires an extraneous axiom.
C) Because the premises are false.
D) Because it is a conditional syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Essential entailment means the conclusion must follow solely from the premises without external principles; the syllogism of equality fails this test.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'The world is created. Every created thing has a creator. Therefore, the world has a creator.' Is this an essential entailment? Why?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Obvious and Non-Obvious Entailment, and the Truth of Premises
Covers obvious/non-obvious entailment and false premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of qiyas covers both perfect and imperfect syllogisms, and why premises can be false.

Core Idea
The definition of syllogism is about logical form, not the truth of premises.

The commentary further clarifies two points: first, the entailment can be either obvious (like in the first figure) or non-obvious (other figures), so the definition includes both perfect and imperfect syllogisms. Second, the phrase 'if accepted' indicates that the premises do not have to be true; even false premises can form a valid syllogism. This lesson explores why these aspects are important for a comprehensive definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the types of entailment
The author says entailment can be 'bayan' (obvious) or 'ghayr bayan' (non-obvious). The first figure is perfect because entailment is obvious; other figures require reduction.
2
Link to the definition
The definition uses the broad term 'luzum' (entailment) to cover both, so all figures are included.
3
Examine the phrase 'after acceptance'
The definition says 'when accepted, entail...'. This means the syllogism works hypothetically: if you accept the premises (even if false), the conclusion follows logically.
4
Apply the condition
Consider the example: 'Man is a stone, every stone is inanimate.' Even though the premises are false, the form is valid. So the definition includes such arguments, focusing on formal validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستدرك كما قال شارح المطالع، أو احتراز عن كون "من" تبعيضية كما صرح به السيد المحقق في "شرح المواقف"، أو أورد ليصح تعلق "من" به، كما صرح العلامة التفتازاني. من قضايا إلخ: المراد بـ "القضايا" ما فوق الواحد، فلا يكون القضية الواحدة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها قياسا يلزم إلخ ينبغي أن يراد باللزوم في قوله: "يلزم" اللزوم الذاتي، كما هو مصرّح في التعريف المشهور هنا: قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلّمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر؛ فيخرج ما يستلزم قولا آخر لذاته، بل بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية كما في قياس المساوات، وهو ما يتركب من قضيتين متعلق محمول إحداهما يكون موضوعا لأخرى كقولنا: أ مساوٍ لـ ب، وب مساوٍ لـ ج؛ فهما يستلزمان أن أ مساو لـ ج، لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة أن مساوي المساوي مساو، حتى لو لم يتحقق لم ينتج شيئا. ثم اعلم أن المراد باللزوم في قوله: "يلزم" أعم من اللازم البين وغير البين؛ ليندرج فيه القياس الكامل، وهو الشكل الأول، وغير الكامل وهو باقي الأشكال. بعد تسليم إلخ إشارة إلى أن مقدمات القياس لا تحب أن تكون مسلّمة في أنفسها، بل إنها وإن كانت كاذبة منكرة لكن هي بحيث لو سُلّمت لزم عنها قول آخر؛ فيدخل في التعريف القياس الصادق المقدمات وغيره كقولنا الإنسان حجر وكل حجر جماد؛ فإن هاتين القضيتين وإن كانتا كاذبتين إلا أنهما بحيث لو سلّمتها لزم عنها أن كل إنسان جماد
Additional consideration: as the commentator of al-Matali' said, or as a precaution against 'min' being partitive as expressed by al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq in Sharh al-Mawaqif, or introduced to validate the attachment of 'min' to it, as expressed by al-Allama al-Taftazani. 'From propositions etc.': what is meant by 'propositions' is more than one, so a single proposition entailing its converse or the converse of its contrary is not a syllogism. 'Entails etc.': it should be understood by 'entails' in his statement the essential entailment, as is declared in the famous definition here: 'A statement composed of statements which, when accepted, entail by themselves another statement'; so it excludes what entails another statement not by itself, but through an extraneous premise, as in the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions such that the predicate of one is the subject of the other, like our statement: 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C'; these two entail that A is equal to C, but not by themselves, rather through the fact that the equal of the equal is equal, such that if it were not established, nothing would be produced. Then know that by 'entails' in his statement is meant the entailment that is broader than the obvious and non-obvious, so that it includes the perfect syllogism, which is the first figure, and the imperfect, which are the remaining figures. 'After acceptance etc.' is an indication that the premises of the syllogism do not have to be accepted in themselves; rather, even if they are false and denied, they are such that if they were accepted, they would entail another statement; thus the definition includes the syllogism with true premises and others, like our statement: 'Man is a stone, and every stone is inanimate'; these two propositions, though false, are such that if you accept them, they entail that every man is inanimate.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the previous definition of qiyas. It clarifies three key points. First, the word 'qadaya' (propositions) must refer to more than one, so a single proposition that implies its converse is not a syllogism. Second, the entailment (luzum) in the definition must be essential (dhati), not through an external premise. The example given is the 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat): 'A equals B, B equals C' only entails 'A equals C' via the axiom that equals of equals are equal. Without that axiom, the entailment fails, so it is not a bona fide syllogism as per the strict definition. Third, the entailment can be obvious or non-obvious, so both perfect (first figure) and imperfect (other figures) syllogisms are included. Additionally, the premises do not need to be true; even false premises can form a syllogism as long as they hypothetically entail a conclusion. The example of 'Man is a stone' and 'Every stone is inanimate' shows that a valid syllogism in form can have false premises.

Key Terms
لزوم
بين
غير بين

B) Because entailment can be obvious or non-obvious.
C) Because premises can be false.
D) Because they all have three terms.

Key Takeaway
The definition covers syllogisms with obvious and non-obvious entailment, and both true and false premises, as long as the conclusion follows formally.

The Syllogism
beginner

The Three Terms of a Conjunctive Syllogism
Author defines the three terms and the premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor term, major term, and middle term in any conjunctive categorical syllogism from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
The three terms are the backbone of every categorical syllogism.

In this passage, the author clarifies the essential components of the conjunctive syllogism: the subject of the conclusion (asghar), its predicate (akbar), and the term repeated in both premises (hadd awsat). Understanding these terms is the first step to analyzing any syllogistic proof.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conclusion
The conclusion is the statement to be proved; its subject is the minor term and its predicate is the major term.
2
Find the repeated term
Look for a term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion; that is the middle term.
3
Label the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (shughra); the premise containing the major term is the major premise (kubra).
4
Practice with an example
Work through the given example: 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated' – identify asghar (العالم), akbar (حادث), hadd awsat (متغير).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاقتراني إلخ لما فرغ من تعريف القياس وتقسيمه ما هو إلى قسمين شرع في الأقسام وابتدأ بالاقتراني المركب من الحمليات وهو يشتمل على حدود ثلاثة: - ۱ موضوع المطلوب. ۲- ومحموله. ٣- والمكرر بينهما في المقدمتين؛ فقال: "فصل في القياس الاقتراني". شرطي القياس الشرطي ما لا يكون مركبا من حمليتين سواء كان مركبا من شرطيتين، أو من شرطية وحملية؛ فتسمية الأول بالشرطي ظاهر وأما تسمية المركب من الشرطية والحملية فتسمية الكل باسم الجزء الأعظم. يسمى أصغر؛ لكونه أقل أفرادا في الأغلب ومحموله يسمّى أكبر؛ لكونه أكثر أفرادا غالبا. والقضية التي جعلت جزء قياس يسمّى مقدّمة، والمقدمة التي فيها الأصغر تسمّى صغرى، والتي فيها الأكبر كبرى والجزء الذي تكرر بينهما يسمى حدا أوسط. واقتران الصغرى بالكبرى يسمّى قرينة وضربا والهيأة الحاصلة من كيفية وضع الأوسط عند الأصغر والأكبر يسمّى شكلا، والأشكال أربعة. وجه الضبط أن يقال: الحدّ الأوسط إما محمول الصغرى وموضوع الكبرى كما في قولنا: العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث ينتج "العالم "حادث فهو الشكل الأول. وإن كان محمولا فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني كما تقول: "كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان" فالنتيجة "لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر". وإن كان موضوعا فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث نحو كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الإنسان كاتب" ينتج "بعض الحيوان كاتب". تسمى صغرى إلخ لاشتمالها على الأصغر، وكذلك الكبرى لاشتمالها على الأكبر، والمتكرر بينهما يسمّى حدا أوسط؛ لتوسطه بين طرفي المطلوب من كيفية وضع الأوسط إلخ أي من جهة كون الأوسط محمولا في الصغرى وموضوعا في الكبرى في الشكل الأول، أو محمولا فيهما في الثاني، أو موضوعا فيهما في الثالث، أو عكسا للأول في الرابع. فهو الشكل الأول إلخ إنما وضعت هذه الأشكال على هذا الترتيب؛ لأن الشكل الأول بديهي الإنتاج، أقرب إلى قبول الطبع وتوجّه النفس بالنسبة إلى البواقي أو إلى النظم الطبيعي، وهو الانتقال من الأصغر إلى الأوسط ومنه إلى الأكبر؛ فلا يتغير الأصغر والأكبر عن حاليهما في النتيجة. وهذا النظم إنما هو في الشكل الأول؛ فلهذا وضع في المرتبة الأولى. ثم وضع الشكل الثاني لمشاركة الأول في أشرف مقدمتيه وهي الصغرى المشتملة على موضوع المطلوب الذي هو أشرف من المحمول ثم الثالث لمشاركة الأول في أخص مقدمتيه، وهي الكبرى ثم الرابع لعدم اشتراكه مع الأول أصلا.
Section on the conjunctive syllogism, etc. When he finished defining the syllogism and dividing it into two types, he began with the divisions and started with the conjunctive composed of categorical propositions, which includes three terms: 1- the subject of the sought conclusion, 2- its predicate, and 3- the term repeated between them in the two premises. So he said: 'Section on the conjunctive syllogism.' The conditional syllogism is that which is not composed of two categorical propositions, whether it is composed of two conditionals or of a conditional and a categorical; thus naming the first as conditional is obvious, and as for naming the composite of conditional and categorical, it is naming the whole by the greater part. The subject of the sought conclusion is called the minor term, because it is usually fewer in individuals; its predicate is called the major term, because it is usually more in individuals. The proposition made a part of the syllogism is called a premise; the premise containing the minor term is called the minor premise, and the one containing the major term is called the major premise. The term repeated between them is called the middle term. The conjunction of the minor and major premises is called a 'qarina' and a 'darb' (mood). The form resulting from the manner of the middle term's position relative to the minor and major terms is called a figure, and the figures are four. The way to control it is to say: The middle term is either the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise, as in our statement: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' yields 'The world is originated'; this is the first figure. If the middle term is predicate in both, it is the second figure, as you say: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing from stone is an animal'; the conclusion is 'Nothing from human is stone.' If it is subject in both, it is the third figure, e.g., 'Every human is an animal, and some human is a writer' yields 'Some animal is a writer.' It is called minor premise etc. because it contains the minor term, and likewise the major premise contains the major term. The repeated term is called the middle term because it mediates between the two ends of the sought conclusion. 'From the manner of the middle's position etc.' i.e., in terms of the middle being predicate in the minor and subject in the major in the first figure, or predicate in both in the second, or subject in both in the third, or opposite of the first in the fourth. So it is the first figure etc. These figures are arranged in this order because the first figure is self-evident in its productivity, closer to natural acceptance and mental attention relative to the others, or to the natural order, which is the transition from the minor to the middle and from there to the major; thus the minor and major do not change from their states in the conclusion. This order is only in the first figure; therefore it is placed in the first rank. Then the second figure is placed because it shares with the first in its more honorable premise, which is the minor premise containing the subject of the sought conclusion, which is more honorable than the predicate. Then the third due to sharing with the first in the more specific premise, which is the major premise. Then the fourth due to having no sharing with the first at all.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the section heading 'Fasl fi al-qiyas al-iqtirani' (Section on the Conjunctive Syllogism). The commentator explains that the conjunctive syllogism is composed of categorical propositions and involves three terms: the subject of the desired conclusion (asghar), its predicate (akbar), and the term repeated in both premises (hadd awsat). The minor term is usually less extensive in individuals, the major term more extensive. The propositions are called premises; the one containing the minor is the minor premise (shughra), the one containing the major is the major premise (kubra). The repeated term is the middle term. The combination of the two premises is called a qarina or darb (mood). The arrangement of the middle term relative to the minor and major defines the figure (shakl). The author enumerates four figures: In the first figure, the middle is predicate of the minor and subject of the major. Example: 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated, so the world is originated.' In the second figure, the middle is predicate in both premises. Example: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, so no human is a stone.' In the third figure, the middle is subject in both. Example: 'Every human is an animal, some human is a writer, so some animal is a writer.' The fourth figure is the reverse of the first, though not explicitly exemplified here. The figures are ordered: first figure is most natural and self-evident; second shares its minor premise (honorable); third shares its major premise (specific); fourth shares nothing. This ordering is based on natural progression from minor to middle to major.

Key Terms
أصغر
أكبر
حد أوسط
صغرى
كبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the middle term in the syllogism 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated'?

B) Changing
C) Originated

Key Takeaway
A conjunctive categorical syllogism consists of exactly three terms: the minor (subject of conclusion), major (predicate of conclusion), and middle (term repeated in premises).

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Four Figures of the Conjunctive Syllogism
Author defines the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which figure a given conjunctive syllogism belongs to based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The figure determines the validity and naturalness of a syllogism.

After explaining the terms, the author defines the four figures by the position of the middle term relative to the minor and major terms. The first figure is the most natural and self-evident; the others are derived from it. This lesson will help you classify any syllogism into its figure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first figure
The middle term is predicate of the minor premise and subject of the major premise. Example: 'The world (minor) is changing (middle, predicated of minor); everything changing (middle, subject of major) is originated (major).'
2
Identify the second figure
The middle term is predicate of both premises. Example: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal.'
3
Identify the third figure
The middle term is subject of both premises. Example: 'Every human is an animal; some human is a writer.'
4
Identify the fourth figure
The fourth figure is the reverse of the first: middle is subject of minor and predicate of major. (Not exemplified in passage but defined.)
5
Apply the classification
For any given syllogism, write down the premises, mark the middle term, and check its position in each premise.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the section heading 'Fasl fi al-qiyas al-iqtirani' (Section on the Conjunctive Syllogism). The commentator explains that the conjunctive syllogism is composed of categorical propositions and involves three terms: the subject of the desired conclusion (asghar), its predicate (akbar), and the term repeated in both premises (hadd awsat). The minor term is usually less extensive in individuals, the major term more extensive. The propositions are called premises; the one containing the minor is the minor premise (shughra), the one containing the major is the major premise (kubra). The repeated term is the middle term. The combination of the two premises is called a qarina or darb (mood). The arrangement of the middle term relative to the minor and major defines the figure (shakl). The author enumerates four figures: In the first figure, the middle is predicate of the minor and subject of the major. Example: 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated, so the world is originated.' In the second figure, the middle is predicate in both premises. Example: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, so no human is a stone.' In the third figure, the middle is subject in both. Example: 'Every human is an animal, some human is a writer, so some animal is a writer.' The fourth figure is the reverse of the first, though not explicitly exemplified here. The figures are ordered: first figure is most natural and self-evident; second shares its minor premise (honorable); third shares its major premise (specific); fourth shares nothing. This ordering is based on natural progression from minor to middle to major.

Key Terms
شكل
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث
الشكل الرابع
قرينة
ضرب

Checkpoint

A) Subject of the minor and predicate of the major
B) Predicate of both premises
C) Subject of both premises
D) Predicate of the minor and subject of the major

Key Takeaway
The four figures are defined by the position of the middle term: first (middle predicate of minor, subject of major), second (predicate in both), third (subject in both), fourth (reverse of first).
