The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Defines the four figures of categorical syllogism and explains their arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism by locating the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The four figures depend only on where the middle term sits in the two premises.

We have learned about the parts of a syllogism: minor term, major term, and middle term. Now we examine how the arrangement of the middle term defines four distinct figures. Each figure has its own validity conditions, and the first figure is the most natural and noblest. This lesson will show you how to recognize each figure from examples in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement that follows from the premises. In the example 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated', the conclusion is 'the world is originated'.
2
Extract the minor and major terms
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (asghar), and the predicate is the major term (akbar). Here, 'the world' is minor, 'originated' is major.
3
Locate the middle term
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It is 'changing' in the first premise (predicate of minor) and 'changing' in the second premise (subject of major).
4
Determine the figure from the middle term's position
If middle is predicate in minor and subject in major, it is first figure. Verify by checking each of the four possibilities from the passage.
The four possibilities: 1st: predicate in minor, subject in major; 2nd: predicate in both; 3rd: subject in both; 4th: subject in minor, predicate in major.
5
Practise with other figures
For the second figure, middle term 'human' is predicate in both premises. For third, 'human' is subject in both. For fourth, 'human' is subject in minor and predicate in major. Each yields a valid syllogism in the passage.
The examples given in the passage are: 2nd: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, therefore no human is a stone'; 3rd: 'Every human is an animal, some humans are writers, therefore some animals are writers'; 4th: 'Every human is an animal, some writers are humans, therefore some animals are writers'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمّى أصغر؛ لكونه أقل أفرادا في الأغلب ومحموله يسمّى أكبر؛ لكونه أكثر أفرادا غالبا. والقضية التي جعلت جزء قياس يسمّى مقدّمة، والمقدمة التي فيها الأصغر تسمّى صغرى، والتي فيها الأكبر كبرى والجزء الذي تكرر بينهما يسمى حدا أوسط. واقتران الصغرى بالكبرى يسمّى قرينة وضربا والهيأة الحاصلة من كيفية وضع الأوسط عند الأصغر والأكبر يسمّى شكلا، والأشكال أربعة. وجه الضبط أن يقال: الحدّ الأوسط إما محمول الصغرى وموضوع الكبرى كما في قولنا: العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث ينتج "العالم حادث" فهو الشكل الأول. وإن كان محمولا فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني كما تقول: "كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان" فالنتيجة "لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر". وإن كان موضوعا فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث نحو كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الإنسان كاتب" ينتج "بعض الحيوان كاتب". وإن كان موضوعا في الصغرى ومحمولا في الكبرى فهو الشكل الرابع نحو قولنا: "كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الكاتب إنسان" ينتج "بعض الحيوان كاتب". فصل وأشرف الأشكال من الأربعة الشكل الأول؛ ولذلك كان إنتاجه بينا بديهيا يسبق الذهن فيه إلى النتيجة سبقا طبعيا من دون حاجة إلى فكر وتأمل.
It is called 'minor' because it is usually fewer in individuals, and its predicate is called 'major' because it is usually more in individuals. The proposition that is made a part of a syllogism is called a 'premise'; the premise containing the minor is called 'minor premise', and that containing the major is called 'major premise'; and the term repeated between them is called 'middle term'. The conjunction of the minor with the major is called 'conjunction' or 'mood'; and the configuration resulting from the manner of placing the middle term relative to the minor and the major is called 'figure'; and the figures are four. The way to grasp it is to say: The middle term is either the predicate of the minor and the subject of the major, as in our statement: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated', yielding 'The world is originated' – that is the first figure. Or it is the predicate in both, then it is the second figure, as you say: 'Every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal', so the conclusion is 'No human is a stone'. Or it is the subject in both, then it is the third figure, as: 'Every human is an animal, and some humans are writers', yielding 'Some animals are writers'. Or it is the subject in the minor and the predicate in the major, then it is the fourth figure, as our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and some writers are humans', yielding 'Some animals are writers'. Section: The most noble of the four figures is the first figure; therefore its production is evident and self-evident, the mind preceding to its conclusion naturally without need for thought and reflection.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism. It begins by explaining the terms 'minor', 'major', 'premise', and 'middle term'. The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, and the major is the predicate. The figure is defined by the position of the middle term in the two premises. Four possibilities are enumerated: middle term as predicate of minor and subject of major (first figure), predicate in both (second), subject in both (third), subject in minor and predicate in major (fourth). Each is illustrated with an example from the Islamic theological/logical context: the first figure example uses 'the world is changing' and 'everything changing is originated' to conclude 'the world is originated', a classic kalam argument. The second figure uses humans and stones; the third uses humans and writers; the fourth also uses humans and writers. The passage then states that the first figure is the most noble because its conclusion follows obviously and naturally, without need for reflection. This sets up the subsequent discussion of its conditions.

Conditions and Productive Moods of the First Figure
Explains the two conditions and enumerates the four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism and determine which premise combinations satisfy them.

Core Idea
Only four of sixteen premise combinations produce a valid first-figure syllogism — the conditions filter the rest.

The first figure is the most powerful and self-evident in categorical logic, but it is not endlessly permissive. To produce a certain conclusion, both premises must satisfy two strict conditions: the minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. This lesson unpacks why these conditions hold and how they eliminate twelve of the sixteen possible premise pairs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Locate the statement of conditions in the Arabic: إيجاب الصغرى (affirmative minor) and كلية الكبرى (universal major). Note the author's emphasis that both are required.
The conditions are directly stated in the first part of the passage.
2
Understand the rationale
For each condition, reason why it is necessary. If the minor is negative, the minor term may not be included under the middle term, so no conclusion about the minor applies. If the major is particular, the judgment may not cover all instances, so the conclusion lacks certainty.
3
Enumerate the 16 possible moods
List the four types (A, E, I, O) for each premise. The minor can be one of four, the major also four, giving 4×4 = 16 combinations.
A = universal affirmative, E = universal negative, I = particular affirmative, O = particular negative.
4
Apply the conditions to drop invalid moods
First, drop all moods where the minor is negative (E or O) — this eliminates 8 moods. From the remaining 8 (all with affirmative minor), drop those where the major is particular (I or O) — this eliminates 4 more. The remaining 4 are productive.
The passage explicitly states that the conditions drop 12 in total.
5
Identify the four productive moods
The four surviving moods have: minor affirmative, major universal. They are traditionally named Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), and Ferio (EIO). Notice that the minor is always affirmative (A or I) and the major universal (A or E).
The passage does not name them but implies they are the four with the conditions satisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل وأشرف الأشكال من الأربعة الشكل الأول؛ ولذلك كان إنتاجه بينا بديهيا يسبق الذهن فيه إلى النتيجة سبقا طبعيا من دون حاجة إلى فكر وتأمل. وله شرائط وضروب. أما الشرائط فاثنان أحدهما إيجاب الصغرى، وثانيهما: كلية الكبرى. فإن يفقدا معا أو يفقد أحدهما لا يلزم النتيجة كما يظهر عند التأمل. وأما الضروب فأربعة؛ لأن الاحتمالات في كل شكل ستة عشر؛ لأن الصغرى أربعة والكبرى أيضا أربعة - أعني الموجبة الكلية، والموجبة الجزئية، والسالبة الكلية، والجزئية – والأربعة في الأربعة ستة عشر وأسقط شرائط الشكل الأوّل اثني عشر : وهو الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبريات الأربع والصغرى السالبة الجزئية مع تلك. وأسقط إلخ :أي: أسقط الشرط الأول، وهو إيجاب الصغرى ثمانية حاصلة من ضرب الصغريين السالبتين في الكبريات الأربع واشتراط كلية الكبرى أسقط أربعة حاصلة من ضرب الكبريين الجزئيتين وفي الصغريين الموجبتين؛ فبقيت الضروب المنتجة أربعة.
Section: The most noble of the four figures is the first figure; therefore its production is evident and self-evident, the mind preceding to its conclusion naturally without need for thought and reflection. It has conditions and moods. As for the conditions, they are two: one is the affirmativeness of the minor, and the second is the universality of the major. If both are missing or one is missing, the conclusion does not follow, as appears upon reflection. As for the moods, they are four; because the possibilities in each figure are sixteen: since the minor is four (i.e., universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) and the major also four, and four times four is sixteen. The conditions of the first figure drop twelve: namely, the universal negative minor with the four majors, and the particular negative minor with those. And the rest: i.e., the first condition, which is the affirmativeness of the minor, drops eight resulting from multiplying the two negative minors by the four majors, and the condition of universality of the major drops four resulting from multiplying the two particular majors by the two affirmative minors; so the productive moods remaining are four.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage details the conditions and productive moods of the first figure. It first reiterates that the first figure is the most noble and its conclusion is self-evident. Then it states two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. If either or both are violated, no conclusion follows. The author explains why: if the minor is negative, the minor term is not subsumed under the middle term; if the major is particular, the judgment on the major may not apply to the whole middle. Then it enumerates the possible moods: there are 16 combinations of the four types of propositions (A, E, I, O) for two premises. The conditions eliminate 12: the condition of affirmative minor eliminates 8 (the two negative minors times the four majors), and the condition of universal major eliminates 4 (the two particular majors times the two affirmative minors). Thus 4 productive moods remain. The author does not list them explicitly here but implies they are the four with affirmative minor and universal major. This is a standard analysis in Aristotelian logic.

The Syllogism

Conditions and Valid Moods of the First Figure
Conditions and four valid moods of the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a categorical syllogism is a valid first-figure syllogism by checking that the minor premise is affirmative and the major premise is universal, and determine which of the four productive moods it belongs to.

Core Idea
Two simple conditions (affirmative minor, universal major) eliminate twelve of sixteen premise combinations, leaving only four valid moods.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the most perfect and immediate. But not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. The logicians teach us that only two conditions are needed to guarantee validity, and only four premise pairs make the cut. In this lesson, you'll learn how to apply these conditions to any syllogism you encounter in Islamic reasoning, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure by locating the middle term
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. Check that the middle term appears in both premises as described.
2
Check the quality of the minor premise
The minor premise must be affirmative (either universal affirmative or particular affirmative). If it is negative, the syllogism is invalid for the first figure.
3
Check the quantity of the major premise
The major premise must be universal (either universal affirmative or universal negative). If it is particular, the syllogism is invalid.
4
Determine the mood from the premise types
Once both conditions are satisfied, note the quantity and quality of both premises. This yields one of four moods: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major; (2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major; (4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major.
The conclusion's form corresponds directly: mood 1 gives universal affirmative, mood 2 gives universal negative, mood 3 gives particular affirmative, mood 4 gives particular negative.
5
Verify the conclusion follows
Check that the conclusion's subject is the minor term and its predicate is the major term. The quantity and quality should match the mood. For example, mood 1 yields 'All C are D'.
The text provides examples with letters (C, B, D) and concrete Islamic examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الأول
الأشكال الأربعة وإن كان موضوعا في الصغرى ومحمولا في الكبرى فهو الشكل الرابع نحو قولنا: "كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الكاتب إنسان" ينتج "بعض الحيوان كاتب". فصل وأشرف الأشكال من الأربعة الشكل الأول؛ ولذلك كان إنتاجه بينا بديهيا يسبق الذهن فيه إلى النتيجة سبقا طبعيا من دون حاجة إلى فكر وتأمل. وله شرائط وضروب. أما الشرائط فاثنان أحدهما إيجاب الصغرى، وثانيهما: كلية الكبرى. فإن يفقدا معا أو يفقد أحدهما لا يلزم النتيجة كما يظهر عند التأمل. وأما الضروب فأربعة؛ لأن الاحتمالات في كل شكل ستة عشر؛ لأن الصغرى أربعة والكبرى أيضا أربعة - أعني الموجبة الكلية، والموجبة الجزئية، والسالبة الكلية، والجزئية – والأربعة في الأربعة ستة عشر وأسقط شرائط الشكل الأوّل اثني عشر : وهو الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبريات الأربع والصغرى السالبة الجزئية مع تلك الأربع، وهذه ثمانية والكبرى الموجبة الجزئية والسالبة الجزئية مع الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية والكلية وهذه أربعة فبقي أربعة ضروب منتجة: الضرب الأول مركب من موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى ينتج موجبة كلية نحو: كل ج ب، وكل ب د، ينتج كل ج د. والضرب الثاني مؤلّف من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة كلية نحو: "كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر" ينتج "لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر". والضرب الثالث ملتئم من موجبة جزئية صغرى ومؤجبة كلية كبرى والنتيجة موجبة جزئية نحو: بعض الحيوان فرس، وكل فرس صهال ينتج بعض الحيوان صهال. والضرب الرابع مزدوج من موجبة جزئية صغرى [وسالبة كلية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية نحو: بعض الحيوان فرس، ولا شيء من الفرس بحمار ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بحمار]
The first figure. The four figures: if the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, it is the fourth figure, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some writer is a human' produces 'Some animal is a writer'. Then the noblest of the four figures is the first figure; therefore its production is evident and self-evident, the mind precedes to the conclusion in a natural precedence without need for thought and reflection. It has conditions and moods. As for the conditions, they are two: one is the affirmation of the minor, and the second is the universality of the major. For if both are lost together or one is lost, the conclusion does not follow, as appears upon reflection. As for the moods, they are four; because the possibilities in each figure are sixteen; because the minor is four and the major also four - namely the universal affirmative, the particular affirmative, the universal negative, and the particular negative - and four into four is sixteen. The conditions of the first figure eliminate twelve: that is the universal negative minor with the four majors, and the particular negative minor with those four - these are eight - and the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major with the particular affirmative minor and the universal affirmative minor - these are four. So four productive moods remain: the first mood is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, producing a universal affirmative, like: every C is B, and every B is D, produces every C is D. The second mood is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, producing a universal negative, like: 'Every human is an animal and no animal is a stone' produces 'No human is a stone'. The third mood is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, and the conclusion is a particular affirmative, like: Some animal is a horse, and every horse neighs, produces some animal neighs. The fourth mood is composed of a particular affirmative minor [and a universal negative major, producing a particular negative, like: Some animal is a horse, and no horse is a donkey, produces some animal is not a donkey].
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by defining the four figures based on the position of the middle term, using the fourth figure as an illustration: when the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, we have the fourth figure. The example given is 'Every human is an animal; some writer is a human; therefore some animal is a writer.' Then the author states that the first figure is the noblest because its conclusion is self-evident; the mind grasps it naturally without deliberate thought. The first figure has two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) the major premise must be universal. If either or both are violated, the conclusion does not follow. The author explains that the total number of possible premise combinations in any figure is sixteen (four types of minor × four types of major). In the first figure, the two conditions eliminate twelve of these: eight are eliminated by the minor being negative (both universal and particular), and four are eliminated by the major being particular (both affirmative and negative when the minor is affirmative). This leaves four valid moods. The author then lists each mood, giving its composition and an example. The first mood: universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield a universal affirmative conclusion. The example uses letters (C, B, D) to represent terms. The second mood: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major yield a universal negative conclusion, exemplified with 'human', 'animal', and 'stone'. The third mood: particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield a particular affirmative conclusion, using 'animal', 'horse', and 'neighing'. The fourth mood (partially reconstructed): particular affirmative minor and universal negative major yield a particular negative conclusion, e.g., some animal is a horse, no horse is a donkey, therefore some animal is not a donkey. This systematic presentation allows the student to see how the conditions constrain the valid forms.

شرائط
ضروب
إيجاب الصغرى
كلية الكبرى

D) Minor: Some a is b (affirmative particular); Major: Some b is c (particular affirmative)

Key Takeaway
The first figure of the categorical syllogism requires the minor premise to be affirmative and the major premise to be universal; these conditions eliminate twelve of sixteen possible premise combinations, leaving only four productive moods.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The First Figure: Productive Moods and Conditions
Enumeration of productive moods and conditions of the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure and verify whether a given first-figure syllogism satisfies its three conditions (quality, quantity, modality).

Core Idea
The first figure uniquely yields all four conclusion types, but only if the minor is affirmative and actual, and the major is universal.

In this lesson, we dive into the most celebrated syllogistic form in Islamic logic: the first figure. The author lays out exactly which premise combinations produce valid conclusions and the strict conditions that must hold. Understanding these conditions is crucial for building sound arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
The first figure has three necessary conditions: (1) by quality, the minor premise must be affirmative; (2) by quantity, the major premise must be universal; (3) by modality, the minor premise must be actual (not merely possible). Note that some earlier logicians like al-Farabi disagreed on the third condition.
These conditions are summarised in the passage after the table.
2
Recognize the four productive moods
Out of the 16 possible combinations of premises (4 types of minor × 4 types of major), only four are productive. They are: (minor affirmative universal + major affirmative universal) → universal affirmative; (minor affirmative universal + major negative universal) → universal negative; (minor affirmative particular + major affirmative universal) → particular affirmative; (minor affirmative particular + major negative universal) → particular negative.
The passage lists these as the first, second, third, and fourth moods respectively.
3
Apply the conditions to determine productivity
Given a first-figure syllogism, check the three conditions: Is the minor affirmative? Is the major universal? Is the minor actual? If any condition fails, the syllogism is not productive. The passage marks failures with 'F' or 'F2' in the table.
The table in the Persian commentary visually maps which combinations fail.
4
Understand the modality debate
The passage explains that later scholars insist on the actuality of the minor because if the minor is possible, the middle term may not actually apply to the minor subject, so the judgment does not transfer with certainty. Al-Farabi held that a possible minor is still productive because the minor is subsumed under the middle potentially.
This debate shows that conditions are not universal across all logical schools.

The four [moods], and these are eight, and the universal negative particular major and the particular negative major with the particular positive minor and the universal, and these are four, so four productive moods remain: the first mood is composed of a universal positive minor and a universal positive major, producing a universal positive, e.g., 'Every J is B, and every B is D' yields 'Every J is D.' The second mood is composed of a universal positive minor and a universal negative major, producing a universal negative, e.g., 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone' yields 'No human is a stone.' The third mood is composed of a particular positive minor and a universal positive major, and the conclusion is a particular positive, e.g., 'Some animal is a horse, and every horse is a neighing animal' yields 'Some animal is a neighing animal.' The fourth mood is composed of a particular positive minor, i.e., it is composite. From the Persian commentary called 'Hadiyya Shahjahaniyya': [Know that the cells under the 'minors' column are the divisions of the minor, and on the left side of the 'majors' column are the divisions of the major. Under the 'examples' column are four examples for the minor in four cells, and on the left side of the 'examples' column for the majors in four cells. Now sixteen cells remain, twelve of which are for the non-productive moods, and in each is written the letter F marked with a number, 'F' indicating the failure of the necessary condition for production, and 'F2' indicating the failure of both necessary conditions for production, and four cells remain opposite the minors' examples column, located under the divisions of the majors' column: the conclusions resulting from combining the majors with the minors.] Examples: Table of the first figure: Majors, Minors: universal positive (Every J is B), particular positive (Some J is B), universal negative (No J is B), particular negative (Some J is not B). Universal positive (Every B is A), universal negative (No B is A), particular positive (Some B is A), particular negative (Some B is not A). [Results:] Every J is A, No J is A, Some J is A, Some J is not A. 1 [unclear] [unclear] F2 [unclear] [unclear] F [unclear]. Warning: The production of the universal positive is a property of the first figure, just as the production of all four conclusions is also among its properties. And the possible minor is not productive in this figure, for it has been clarified by what we have mentioned that in this figure, by quality, the minor must be positive, by quantity, the major must be universal, and by modality, the minor must be actual. 'And the possible minor is not productive, etc.' because the first figure has three conditions: first, by quality, it is the positivity of the minor; second, by quantity, it is the universality of the major; third, by modality, it is the actuality of the minor, i.e., the minor must not be possible. And the author mentioned only the first two conditions; he alluded to the third in this context. Its summary is that the later scholars held that in the first figure, by modality, the actuality of the minor is required; this is because if the minor were possible, certainty would not be obtained regarding the transfer of the judgment from the middle to the minor, for the major indicates that everything of which the middle is predicated is subject to the major, and the minor is not actually middle but potentially. And it is possible that it does not emerge from potentiality to actuality; so the judgment does not transfer from it to the minor. And according to al-Farabi's opinion, the possible is productive because the minor is subsumed under the middle.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the first figure of the categorical syllogism, defining its productive moods and specifying its necessary conditions. The author begins by stating that out of the eight possible combinations of premises, only four are productive in the first figure. He then lists these four moods: (1) universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield universal affirmative; (2) universal affirmative minor and universal negative major yield universal negative; (3) particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield particular affirmative; (4) particular affirmative minor and universal negative major yield particular negative. Examples are given using the variables J, B, D for terms, and also with concrete terms like 'human', 'animal', 'stone', 'horse', 'neighing'. A Persian commentary is then inserted that explains a table diagramming the moods. The table maps the four types of minors (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) against four types of majors (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and indicates which combinations produce conclusions and which fail, marking failed ones with 'F' (for one condition failure) or 'F2' (for both conditions failure). Following the table, the author adds a 'tanbih' (warning) that the first figure uniquely produces all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and that the minor premise must be actual (not merely possible). The text then elaborates on the three conditions for the first figure: (1) by quality, the minor must be affirmative; (2) by quantity, the major must be universal; (3) by modality, the minor must be actual (i.e., not a possible proposition). The author notes that the original text mentioned only the first two, but the third is implied. The reason for the third condition is that if the minor is possible, the middle term may not actually apply to the minor subject, so the judgment does not transfer with certainty. However, al-Farabi held that a possible minor is still productive because the minor is subsumed under the middle potentially. This passage thus provides a complete characterization of the first figure's valid moods and the conditions that guarantee their validity.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, characterized by the middle term being the subject in the major premise and the predicate in the minor premise. [Note: Stable across the tradition; the definition remains consistent.]
ضرب (darb): A mood of the syllogism, i.e., a specific combination of premise quantities and qualities that yields a valid conclusion. [Note: Used consistently in Mantiq texts for 'mood' of a syllogism.]
موجبة كلية (mujiba kulliyya): A universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every J is B'). [Note: Standard term in Arabic logic.]
سالبة كلية (saliba kulliyya): A universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No J is B'). [Note: Standard term.]
موجبة جزئية (mujiba juz'iyya): A particular affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Some J is B'). [Note: Standard term.]
صغرى ممكنة (sughra mumkina): A minor premise that is a possible proposition (i.e., asserting possibility, not actuality). [Note: The notion of a 'possible' premise in syllogistic comes from modal logic; al-Farabi's view is noted as divergent.]
فعلية الصغرى (fi'liyya al-sughra): The actuality of the minor premise, meaning it must be a proposition about actual existence, not merely possible. [Note: This condition is emphasized by later logicians; earlier logicians like al-Farabi had different views.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure, what is the conclusion when the minor premise is 'every horse is an animal' and the major is 'no animal is inanimate'?
A) No horse is inanimate.
B) Some horse is inanimate.
C) Every horse is inanimate.
D) Some horse is not inanimate.

Key Takeaway
The first figure is the most powerful syllogistic form; its validity depends on the minor being affirmative and actual, and the major being universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid first-figure categorical syllogism on a fiqh issue, such as: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh (a certain type of beverage) is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Verify that the minor is affirmative, the major is universal, and the minor is actual (i.e., we are speaking of actual intoxicants, not possible ones).
?
Identify why the following first-figure syllogism fails to produce certainty: 'Some possible thing is changing; everything changing is temporal; therefore something that is possible is temporal.' Which condition is violated and why?

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Condition of the Second Figure: Premises Must Differ in Quality
Author states the condition for the second figure and refutes the contrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the quality condition for the second figure and apply it to determine whether a given pair of premises can yield a valid conclusion, using counterexamples to justify the condition's necessity.

Core Idea
In the second figure, one premise must be affirmative and the other negative—both cannot be the same.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms has a specific requirement: the premises must differ in quality. In this lesson, we will examine the condition as stated by the author, see it applied in an example, and understand why violating it leads to inconsistency through counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the second figure
Find where the author states the requirement: 'ويشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثاني بحسب الكيف اختلاف المقدمتين'. The condition is that the two premises must differ in quality—one affirmative, one negative.
2
Examine the initial example of a valid mood
The author gives an example: 'Some animal is rational, and no rational is a braying animal' produces 'Some animal is not a braying animal'. Identify the quality of each premise (affirmative minor, negative major) and the conclusion (negative). This is a valid second figure mood (Festino).
Note that the middle term (rational) is the predicate in both premises.
3
Analyze the refutation of two affirmatives
The author shows why both premises cannot be affirmative. He gives two substitutions: 'Every human is animal, every rational is animal' (truth is affirmative) and 'Every human is animal, every horse is animal' (truth is negative). The same form yields opposite truths, proving the condition necessary.
The 'truth' here refers to the correct conclusion about the terms, not the syllogistic conclusion.
4
Analyze the refutation of two negatives
Similarly, with two negatives: 'No horse is stone' (truth negative) and 'No rational is stone' (truth affirmative) — again inconsistency.
5
Summarize the logical necessity
The condition of differing quality is not arbitrary. It is required to guarantee that the syllogism yields a determinate conclusion. Without it, the form can produce both true affirmatives and true negatives depending on the terms, which is impossible for a valid syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسالبة كلية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا: بعض الحيوان ناطق، ولا شيء من الناطق بناهق فالنتيجة بعض الحيوان ليس بناهق.
فصل: ويشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثاني بحسب الكيف أي: الإيجاب والسلب اختلاف المقدمتين؛ فإن كانت الصغرى موجبة كانت الكبرى سالبة وبالعكس.
ويشترط في إنتاج الشكل إلخ لأنه لو لم يتحقق أحد الشرطين لحصل الاختلاف، وهو صدق القياس تارة مع الإيجاب وأخرى مع السلب. أما في الموجبتين فيصدق "كل إنسان حيوان، وكل ناطق حيوان" والحق الإيجاب. ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا : كل" فرس حيوان كان الحق السلب. وأما في السالبتين فلصدق قولنا: "لا شيء من الفرس بحجر" والحق السلب ولو قلنا فلا شيء من الناطق بحجر كان الحق الإيجاب. وكذلك على تقدير انتفاء الشرط الثاني. أما على تقدير إيجاب الكبرى فلصدق "لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس وبعض الحيوان فرس"، والصادق الإيجاب ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا : وبعض الصاهل "فرس كان الصادق السلب. وأما على تقدير سلبها فكقولنا: "كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الجسم ليس بحيوان الصادق الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى وقلنا: "بعض الحجر ليس بحيوان" كان الحق السلب.
And a universal negative major produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Some animal is rational, and no rational is a braying animal'; so the conclusion is 'Some animal is not a braying animal.'
Section: For the production of the second figure, by quality, i.e., affirmation and negation, the difference of the two premises is required; so if the minor is affirmative, the major must be negative, and vice versa.
'And it is required for the production of the figure, etc.' because if one of the two conditions is not realized, discrepancy occurs, namely the syllogism is true sometimes with affirmation and sometimes with negation. As for the case of two affirmatives, our saying is true: 'Every human is an animal, and every rational is an animal' and the truth is affirmation. But if we change the major to our saying: 'Every horse is an animal', the truth would be negation. As for the case of two negatives, our saying is true: 'No horse is a stone' and the truth is negation, but if we say 'No rational is a stone', the truth would be affirmation. Similarly, in the case of the absence of the second condition: in the case of the major being affirmative, our saying is true: 'No human is a horse, and some animal is a horse' and the truthful is affirmation, and if we change the major to our saying: 'And some neighing animal is a horse', the truthful would be negation. And in the case of the major being negative, as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some body is not an animal', the truthful is affirmation, and if we change the major and say: 'Some stone is not an animal', the truth would be negation.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for valid syllogisms in the second figure. It opens with an example: a particular affirmative minor ('Some animal is rational') combined with a universal negative major ('No rational is a braying animal') yields a particular negative conclusion ('Some animal is not a braying animal'). This is the mood Festino in the second figure. The author then formally states the condition: in the second figure, the two premises must differ in quality—one must be affirmative and the other negative. To demonstrate why this condition is necessary, the author considers what happens if the condition is violated. If both premises are affirmative, one can find true instances where the conclusion would be either true or false depending on the terms, such as 'Every human is an animal, every rational is an animal' (true) versus 'Every human is an animal, every horse is an animal' (false). Similarly, if both premises are negative, one can find counterexamples. The author also discusses cases where the quantity condition (the second condition) is absent, but the focus is on the quality condition. Through these examples, the author shows that without the condition of differing quality, valid syllogisms cannot be guaranteed—the same form can yield both true affirmatives and true negatives, which is inconsistent. Hence, the condition is necessary for the second figure's validity.

The Syllogism

Conditions for a Valid Syllogism in the Second Figure
Author states the conditions for production of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions (on quality and quantity) required for a valid second-figure syllogism and identify violations using examples.

Core Idea
In the second figure, premises must differ in quality and the major must be universal to avoid inconsistency.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has specific conditions for valid production. Unlike the first figure, where the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other, here the middle term is predicate in both. This requires careful control of quality and quantity. In this lesson, we will extract the two conditions from the text and see why they are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality condition
Find in the passage where it states that the two premises must differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. This is the first condition.
The text says: 'يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثاني بحسب الكيف اختلاف المقدمتين'
2
Understand why the condition is needed
If both premises are affirmative, the syllogism may yield both affirmative and negative conclusions depending on the subject matter, leading to inconsistency. The same holds if both are negative. The passage provides examples like 'Every human is an animal, every rational being is an animal' vs. 'Every human is an animal, every horse is an animal' to illustrate.
3
Identify the quantity condition
The major premise must be universal. If it is particular, inconsistency again arises. The text states: 'وبحسب الكمّ: كلية الكبرى، وإلا يلزم الاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج'
4
Apply the conditions to a sample syllogism
Take a second-figure syllogism like: 'Every A is B, no C is B, therefore no A is C'. Check: minor affirmative, major negative → quality condition satisfied. Major is universal → quantity condition satisfied. So it is potentially productive.
If the major were particular, e.g., 'some B is C', the syllogism would be invalid.

أ، وبانضمامه إلى الصغرى انتظم الشكل الأول، وينتج النتيجة المطلوبة. عكس الكبرى إلى: اعلم أن الدليل على الإنتاج المذكور في الضرب الأول من الشكل الثاني أمران: أحدهما: عكس الكبرى، كما ذكر المصنف محله، واختار هذا لكونه أسهل وثانيهما الخُلف، وهو في هذا الشكل أن يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة ويجعل الصغرى؛ لأن نتائج هذا الشكل سالبة؛ فنقيضها وهو الموجبة يصلح لصغرَوِيَّة الشكل الأول، ويجعل كبرى القياس كبرى؛ لأنها لكليتها تصلح لِكُبْرَويَّةِ الشكل الأول؛ فينتظم منهما قياس في الشكل الأول ينتج لا يناقض الصغرى؛ فيقال: لو لم يصدق "لا شيء من ج أ" لصدق "بعض ج أ"، ونضمه إلى الكبرى هكذا بعض ج أ، ولا شيء من أب ينتج من الشكل الأول بعض ج ليس ب، وقد كان الصغرى "كل ج ب" هذا خلف والخلف لا يلزم من الصورة لأنها بديهية الإنتاج، فيكون من المادة وليس من الكبرى؛ لأنها مفروضة الصدق؛ فتعين أن يكون من نقيض النتيجة؛ فيكون محالا، فالنتيجة حقة.
Section: For the production of the second figure, in terms of quality (i.e., affirmation and negation), it is required that the two premises differ; so if the minor is affirmative, the major must be negative, and vice versa. And it is required for the production of the figure, etc., because if one of the two conditions is not realized, then variation occurs, i.e., the syllogism is true sometimes with affirmation and sometimes with negation. As for two affirmatives, 'Every human is an animal, and every rational being is an animal' is true, and the correct conclusion is affirmative. And if we replace the major with 'Every horse is an animal', the correct conclusion is negative. As for two negatives, 'No horse is a stone' is true, and the correct conclusion is negative; and if we say 'No rational being is a stone', the correct conclusion is affirmative. Similarly on the supposition of the absence of the second condition. As for the supposition that the major is affirmative, 'No human is a horse and some animal is a horse' is true, and the correct conclusion is affirmative; and if we replace the major with 'And some neighing thing is a horse', the correct conclusion is negative. As for the supposition that it is negative, as in 'Every human is an animal and some body is not an animal', the correct conclusion is affirmative; and if we replace the major with 'Some stone is not an animal', the correct conclusion is negative. And in terms of quantity (i.e., universality and particularity), the major must be universal; otherwise variation occurs necessitating non-production, i.e., the syllogism is true sometimes with affirmation of the conclusion and sometimes with its negation. And the conclusion of this figure is always negative. Its productive moods are also four: one of them is from two universals with the minor affirmative, producing a universal negative, as in our saying: 'Every J is B and no A is B, therefore no J is A'. The proof of this production is the conversion of the major; for if you convert the major, it becomes 'No A is B', and when joined to the minor, the first figure is formed and produces the desired conclusion. As for conversion of the major: know that the proof for the mentioned production in the first mood of the second figure has two aspects: one is conversion of the major, as the author mentioned in its place, and he chose this because it is easier; the second is reductio (al-khulf), which in this figure is that one takes the contradictory of the conclusion and makes it the minor—because the conclusions of this figure are negative, so their contradictory, which is affirmative, is suitable as a minor for the first figure—and makes the major of the syllogism the major (because its universality makes it suitable as a major for the first figure); then from them a syllogism in the first figure is formed that produces something not contradicting the minor. So it is said: If 'No J is A' were not true, then 'Some J is A' would be true. We join it to the major thus: 'Some J is A, and no A is B' produces from the first figure 'Some J is not B', whereas the minor was 'Every J is B'. This is a contradiction. The contradiction does not follow from the form because it is self-evidently productive, so it must be from the matter, not from the major because it is assumed true; therefore it must be from the contradictory of the conclusion; so it is impossible, and the conclusion is true.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the conditions and productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating the quality condition: the two premises must differ in quality—if the minor is affirmative, the major must be negative, and vice versa. The commentator explains why this condition is necessary: if both premises are affirmative or both negative, the syllogism would sometimes yield a true affirmative conclusion and sometimes a true negative conclusion, resulting in inconsistency. It illustrates with examples from everyday knowledge (e.g., 'Every human is an animal', 'No horse is a stone'). Next, it states the quantity condition: the major premise must be universal; otherwise, the same problem of inconsistency arises. The passage then notes that the conclusion of the second figure is always negative. It identifies four productive moods; the first is presented in detail: both premises universal, minor affirmative, producing a universal negative conclusion, e.g., 'Every J is B, no A is B, therefore no J is A'. The proof is given in two ways: by conversion of the major premise and by reductio (khulf). Conversion of the major (changing 'No A is B' to its converse 'No B is A') allows the syllogism to be reduced to the self-evidently productive first figure. The reductio proof argues that if the conclusion were false, its contradictory (a particular affirmative) would be true; combining that with the original major yields a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the original minor, proving the conclusion must be true. This structure is typical: conditions first, then moods, then proofs.

Key Terms

الكيف
الكم

Key Takeaway
For a second-figure syllogism to be productive, the premises must differ in quality and the major must be universal.

The Syllogism

Productive Moods and Proofs in the Second Figure
Author details the first productive mood and its proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first productive mood of the second figure and reconstruct its proof using conversion and reductio.

Core Idea
The first mood of the second figure (Cesare) is proven by converting the major to the first figure or by reductio ad absurdum.

After establishing the conditions, the text presents the four productive moods of the second figure. The first mood is given special attention with two proofs: conversion of the major premise and reductio (khulf). These proofs demonstrate how the second figure's validity ultimately rests on the self-evident first figure. In this lesson, we will analyze the first mood and its proofs, which are essential for understanding how any second-figure syllogism can be reduced to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first mood
The first mood has both premises universal, the minor affirmative, and produces a universal negative conclusion. The pattern: 'Every J is B, no A is B, therefore no J is A.' This is mood Cesare.
The text calls it 'أحدها من كليتين والصغرى موجبة، ينتج سالبة كلية'
2
Understand proof by conversion
Convert the major premise 'no A is B' to 'no B is A' (simple conversion). Then combine with the minor 'every J is B' to form a first-figure syllogism: 'Every J is B, no B is A, therefore no J is A.' This is Celarent in the first figure, which is self-evidently valid.
The text says: 'عكس الكبرى؛ فإنك إذا عكست الكبرى صار لا شيء من ب أ، وبانضمامه إلى الصغرى انتظم الشكل الأول'
3
Understand proof by reductio (khulf)
Assume the conclusion is false, so 'some J is A' is true. Combine this with the original major (no A is B) to get 'some J is not B' in the first figure. But the minor says 'every J is B', a contradiction. Therefore the assumption is false, and the original conclusion must be true.
The text details the reductio: 'يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة ويجعل الصغرى...'
4
Generalize to other moods
The other three moods of the second figure can be proven similarly, either by conversion or by conversion plus per accidens. The key is that all reduce to the first figure, establishing the universal validity of the second figure under its conditions.

Moods and Conditions of the Third Figure
Author enumerates moods and conditions of third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six productive moods of the third figure and verify whether a given syllogism satisfies the conditions for a valid third-figure conclusion.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises; only particular conclusions follow.

The third figure of categorical syllogism has a unique structure: the middle term is the subject in both premises. This limits the conclusions to particulars. The author lists six valid moods and the key conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative and one premise must be universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In the third figure, the middle term (B) is the subject in both premises. For example, in 'Every B is J' and 'Every B is A', B is the middle term.
2
Check the condition
Verify that the minor premise (the premise containing the subject of the conclusion) is affirmative and that at least one of the two premises is universal. This is required for a valid conclusion.
3
Match the mood
Compare the types of premises (A, E, I, O) with the six moods listed. For instance, if both premises are universal affirmative (A), it is the first mood.
4
Derive the conclusion
Apply the rule: the conclusion is always particular (I or O) with the extreme terms (J and A) as subject and predicate. For the first mood, the conclusion is 'Some J is A'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الثالث الضرب الثاني من موجبة كلية كبرى وسالبة كليةٍ صغرى كقولنا: "لا شيء من ج ب وكل أ ب" ينتج "لا شيء من ج .أ. والدليل على الإنتاج عكس الصغرى وجعلها كبرى ثم عكس النتيجة. الضرب الثالث من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة كقولك: " بعض ج ب، ولا شيء من أب، فليس بعض ج أ. الضرب الرابع من سالبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، تقول: "بعض ج ليس ب، وكل أب، فبعض ج ليس أ". فصل : شرط إنتاج الشكل الثالث كون الصغرى موجبة وكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية؛ فضروبه الناتجة ستة أحدها: "كل" ب ج ، وكل ب أ، فبعض ج أ". وثانيها: "كل" ب" ج، ولا شيء من ب أ ، فبعض جليس .. وثالثها: "بعض ب ج، وكل ب أ، فبعض ج .أ" . ورابعها بعض ب ج ، ولا شيء من ب أ، فبعض ج ليس أ". وخامسها كل ب ج ، وبعض ب أ ، فبعض ج .أ". وسادسها: "كل ب ج، وبعض ب ليس أ، فبعض ج ليس أ". جدول شكل ثالث كبريات موجبة كلية سالبة كلية موجبة جزئية سالبة جزئية صغريات كل ب أ لا شيء من ب أ بعض ب أ بعض ب ليس أ بعض ج أ بعض ج ليس أ بعض ج أ بعض ج ليس أ أمثلة موجبة كلية كل ب ج موجبة جزئية بعض ب ج سالبة كلية لا شيء من ب ج بعض ج أ بعض ج ليس أ ف ۲ 1 سالبة جزئية بعض ج ليس ب ۲ فا مع كلية الصغرى، أو
The third figure: The second mood: from a universal affirmative major and a universal negative minor, such as our saying: 'No J is B, and every A is B' yields 'No J is A'. And the proof for the production is by converting the minor and making it major then converting the conclusion. The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yields a negative, as you say: 'Some J is B, and no A is B, so some J is not A.' The fourth mood: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, yields a particular negative: you say: 'Some J is not B, and every A is B, so some J is not A.' Section: The condition for the production of the third figure is that the minor be affirmative and that one of the two premises be universal. Its productive moods are six. First: 'Every B is J, and every B is A, so some J is A.' Second: 'Every B is J, and no B is A, so some J is not A.' Third: 'Some B is J, and every B is A, so some J is A.' Fourth: 'Some B is J, and no B is A, so some J is not A.' Fifth: 'Every B is J, and some B is A, so some J is A.' Sixth: 'Every B is J, and some B is not A, so some J is not A.' Table of the third figure: Major premises: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Minor premises: every B is A, no B is A, some B is A, some B is not A. Examples: universal affirmative: every B is J; particular affirmative: some B is J; universal negative: no B is J; particular negative: some B is not J. (Table entries: some J is A, some J is not A, some J is A, some J is not A; then corresponding examples with condition of universal minor, or...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage expounds the third figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-thālith). The author first lists four initial moods (though the text begins with the second mood, implying the first was covered earlier or is the table's first row). He gives the second mood: universal affirmative major and universal negative minor, concluding a universal negative, with proof by conversion. The third mood: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, concluding a particular negative. The fourth mood: particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, concluding a particular negative. Then comes a new section (faṣl) stating the conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism: the minor premise (ṣughrā) must be affirmative, and one of the two premises must be universal. Under these conditions, six productive moods result, which the author enumerates: (1) كل ب ج، كل ب أ → بعض ج أ (every B is J, every B is A → some J is A). (2) كل ب ج، لا شيء من ب أ → بعض ج ليس أ (every B is J, no B is A → some J is not A). (3) بعض ب ج، كل ب أ → بعض ج أ (some B is J, every B is A → some J is A). (4) بعض ب ج، لا شيء من ب أ → بعض ج ليس أ (some B is J, no B is A → some J is not A). (5) كل ب ج، بعض ب أ → بعض ج أ (every B is J, some B is A → some J is A). (6) كل ب ج، بعض ب ليس أ → بعض ج ليس أ (every B is J, some B is not A → some J is not A). He then provides a table (jadwal) summarizing the valid combinations of major and minor premises and their conclusions. The table uses logical variables (ج، ب، أ) and lists examples. For instance, with a universal affirmative major (كل ب أ) and a universal affirmative minor (كل ب ج), the conclusion is some J is A (some ج is أ). The table also shows that some moods require the minor premise to be universal (مع كلية الصغرى) as a condition. The commentary should note that in third figure, the middle term (ب) is subject in both premises, and only particular conclusions are possible. An Islamic example: 'Every scholar (B) is human (J). Every scholar (B) is fallible (A). Therefore, some humans (J) are fallible (A).' Or 'Every intoxicant (B) is forbidden (J). Every intoxicant (B) is harmful (A). Therefore, some forbidden things (J) are harmful (A).'

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
ضرب
عكس الصغرى
ناتجة

Checkpoint

D) The conclusion must be universal.

Key Takeaway
In the third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises; the conclusion is always particular, and the minor must be affirmative with at least one universal premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Islamic examples, construct a valid third-figure syllogism where the minor premise is particular affirmative and the major is universal affirmative. Identify the middle term and state the conclusion.
?
Check the following argument: 'Every scholar (B) is knowledgeable (A). Some humans (J) are scholars (B). Therefore, some humans (J) are knowledgeable (A).' Is this a valid third-figure syllogism? Explain why or why not.

The Table of the Third Figure
Author provides a table summarizing the productive moods of the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to read and construct a table of the third figure to quickly determine valid conclusions given major and minor premise types.

Core Idea
The table summarizes all valid combinations of premises in the third figure; each cell shows the conclusion type.

The author provides a compact table summarizing the six productive moods. The table rows correspond to minor premise types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and columns to major premise types (same). By cross-referencing, you can instantly see the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rows and columns
The rows represent minor premises (labeled by type: كل ب ج, لا شيء من ب ج, بعض ب ج, بعض ج ليس ب). The columns represent major premises (similar types with A instead of J).
2
Find the intersection
Locate the cell where the given minor and major premise types meet. For example, minor = universal affirmative, major = universal affirmative gives the top-left cell.
3
Read the conclusion
The cell tells whether the conclusion is 'some J is A' (I) or 'some J is not A' (O). For the example, the conclusion is 'some J is A'.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Suppose we have 'Every scholar (B) is human (J)' (universal affirmative minor) and 'Every scholar (B) is fallible (A)' (universal affirmative major). The table gives 'some humans (J) are fallible (A)'.

The third figure: The second mood: from a universal affirmative major and a universal negative minor, such as our saying: 'No J is B, and every A is B' yields 'No J is A'. And the proof for the production is by converting the minor and making it major then converting the conclusion. The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yields a negative, as you say: 'Some J is B, and no A is B, so some J is not A.' The fourth mood: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, yields a particular negative: you say: 'Some J is not B, and every A is B, so some J is not A.' Section: The condition for the production of the third figure is that the minor be affirmative and that one of the two premises be universal. Its productive moods are six. First: 'Every B is J, and every B is A, so some J is A.' Second: 'Every B is J, and no B is A, so some J is not A.' Third: 'Some B is J, and every B is A, so some J is A.' Fourth: 'Some B is J, and no B is A, so some J is not A.' Fifth: 'Every B is J, and some B is A, so some J is A.' Sixth: 'Every B is J, and some B is not A, so some J is not A.' Table of the third figure: Major premises: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Minor premises: every B is A, no B is A, some B is A, some B is not A. Examples: universal affirmative: every B is J; particular affirmative: some B is J; universal negative: no B is J; particular negative: some B is not J. Commentary
This passage expounds the third figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-thālith). The author first lists four initial moods (though the text begins with the second mood, implying the first was covered earlier or is the table's first row). He gives the second mood: universal affirmative major and universal negative minor, concluding a universal negative, with proof by conversion. The third mood: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, concluding a particular negative. The fourth mood: particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, concluding a particular negative. Then comes a new section (faṣl) stating the conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism: the minor premise (ṣughrā) must be affirmative, and one of the two premises must be universal. Under these conditions, six productive moods result, which the author enumerates: (1) كل ب ج، كل ب أ → بعض ج أ (every B is J, every B is A → some J is A). (2) كل ب ج، لا شيء من ب أ → بعض ج ليس أ (every B is J, no B is A → some J is not A). (3) بعض ب ج، كل ب أ → بعض ج أ (some B is J, every B is A → some J is A). (4) بعض ب ج، لا شيء من ب أ → بعض ج ليس أ (some B is J, no B is A → some J is not A). (5) كل ب ج، بعض ب أ → بعض ج أ (every B is J, some B is A → some J is A). (6) كل ب ج، بعض ب ليس أ → بعض ج ليس أ (every B is J, some B is not A → some J is not A). He then provides a table (jadwal) summarizing the valid combinations of major and minor premises and their conclusions. The table uses logical variables (ج، ب، أ) and lists examples. For instance, with a universal affirmative major (كل ب أ) and a universal affirmative minor (كل ب ج), the conclusion is some J is A (some ج is أ). The table also shows that some moods require the minor premise to be universal (مع كلية الصغرى) as a condition. The commentary should note that in third figure, the middle term (ب) is subject in both premises, and only particular conclusions are possible. An Islamic example: 'Every scholar (B) is human (J). Every scholar (B) is fallible (A). Therefore, some humans (J) are fallible (A).' Or 'Every intoxicant (B) is forbidden (J). Every intoxicant (B) is harmful (A). Therefore, some forbidden things (J) are harmful (A).'

Conditions and Valid Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author states conditions for fourth figure and provides its valid moods in a table.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given categorical syllogism belongs to the fourth figure and check whether it satisfies the condition for validity.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is valid only if both premises are affirmative or differ in quality with one universal.

The fourth figure is the most symmetrical of the four figures, but its validity conditions are stricter. The author gives two conditions that cover all productive moods, and then presents a table that makes the valid and invalid combinations immediately visible. Mastering this table will allow you to recognize fourth-figure syllogisms in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check that the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise. In the table, the major premise always has A as subject, the minor premise has B as subject, and the middle term is B (predicate of major, subject of minor).
If the terms are arranged differently, it is not the fourth figure.
2
Verify the condition
The two allowed cases: (1) both premises are affirmative (both positive), or (2) they differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one premise is universal (كلية). If neither condition holds, the syllogism is invalid.
Condition (2) is disjunctive: either one is universal affirmative and the other universal negative, or one universal affirmative and the other particular negative, etc.
3
Use the table to check validity
Locate the major premise in the column headers (كل أ ب, لا شيء من أ ب, بعض أ ب, بعض أ ليس ب) and the minor premise in the row headers (كل ب ج, لا شيء من ب ج, بعض ب ج, بعض ب ليس ج). The intersection shows the conclusion if valid, or a blank/invalid marker.
For example, major كل أ ب and minor كل ب ج gives the conclusion بعض ج أ (some J is A).
4
Understand the three proof methods
The author mentions that the productive moods can be proven by reductio (الخلف), by reversing the order of premises (عكس الترتيب) then drawing the conclusion, or by converting both premises (عكس المقدمتين). This shows that the fourth figure can be reduced to the first figure.
These methods are not needed to use the table, but they connect the fourth figure to earlier material.

Examples: universal affirmative: every B is J, some J is A, some J is not A, some J is A, some J is not A; particular affirmative: some B is J; universal negative: no B is J, no J is A, some J is not A; particular negative: some B is not J, some J is not A.

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rābiʿ). The author gives its conditions: either both premises are affirmative, or they differ in quality with one being universal. Under these conditions, eight productive moods exist. The proofs can be by reductio (bi-l-khalf), by reversing the order of premises (bi-ʿaks al-tartīb) then drawing the conclusion, or by converting both premises (bi-ʿaks al-muqaddimatayn). The passage then presents a table (jadwal) that lays out the valid combinations. The table has four columns for major premises (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and four rows for minor premises (every A is B, no A is B, some A is B, some A is not B). For each intersection, the table shows the resulting conclusion. For example, with a universal affirmative major (كل أ ب) and a universal affirmative minor (كل ب ج), the conclusion is بعض ج أ (some J is A). With a universal negative major (لا شيء من أ ب) and a universal affirmative minor, the conclusion is لا شيء من ج أ (no J is A). The table also indicates that some combinations are invalid (e.g., particular negative major with particular negative minor yields no conclusion?). The middle term is B (human). Actually careful: in fourth figure, the middle term is predicate of major and subject of minor? Standard: fourth figure: middle term is predicate of major and subject of minor. But the table uses A as subject of major? So the middle term is B, which is subject of minor and predicate of major. Example: Major: Every human (A) is mortal (B). Minor: Every mortal (B) is created (J). So an Islamic example: 'Every human (A) is created (B). Every created thing (B) is contingent (J). Therefore, some contingent things (J) are human (A).'

Fourth Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author states condition for fourth figure and lists productive moods with a table.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a productive fourth-figure syllogism and determine which moods are valid using the provided table.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires either both premises affirmative or differing in quality with one universal.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms has limited utility but specific conditions for validity. In this passage, the author states the two conditions and presents a table showing which premise combinations yield conclusions. Your task is to internalize this condition and learn to read the table to identify productive moods.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
The text states: 'إما إيجاب المقدمتين أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما' – either both premises are affirmative, or they differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) while one is universal. This is the necessary condition for the fourth figure to produce a valid conclusion.
The condition is simpler than it seems: only when both premises have the same quality (affirmative) or when they differ but at least one is universal do we get valid moods.
2
Locate the table structure
The table has major premises (الكبريات) listed horizontally: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Minor premises (الصغريات) are listed vertically: every A is B, no A is B, some A is B, some A is not B. The major premise is about B and J, the minor about A and B, so the middle term is B.
The table shows both valid and invalid moods (الساقطات والباقيات). You must identify which cells contain conclusions – those are the productive moods.
3
Extract valid moods
From the examples: when major is 'كل ب ج' (universal affirmative) and minor is 'بعض أ ب' (particular affirmative), the conclusion is 'بعض ج أ' – valid. When major is 'لا شيء من ب ج' (universal negative) and minor is 'كل أ ب' (universal affirmative), the conclusion is 'لا شيء من ج أ' – valid. Use the condition to confirm why these work.
Not all combinations yield a conclusion. The table includes all four major types and four minor types, but only eight moods are productive (ضروبه الناتجة ثمانية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الرابع

The Fourth Figure: Its fifth mood: every B is J, and some B is A, therefore some J is A. Its sixth mood: every B is J, and some B is not A, therefore some J is not A. Section: The conditions for the fourth figure's productivity, despite their abundance and lack of utility, are mentioned in the detailed works, so we are not obliged to mention them, and likewise the conditions of the other figures with respect to modality cannot be borne by treatises like this one to explain them. Then the fourth figure: the condition for the fourth figure's productivity is one of two things: either both premises are affirmative, or they differ in quality while one is universal. Its productive moods are eight by contraposition and by reversing the order, then the conclusion, or by conversion of the premises. And the invalid and valid moods with their conclusions become clear to you from this mirror. Commentary
This passage defines the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. It begins by listing two specific moods: the fifth mood where the minor premise is particular affirmative (Some B is A) and the sixth where it is particular negative (Some B is not A). The author then notes that the detailed conditions for the fourth figure, though numerous, are not needed here and are found in larger works. Subsequently, the author states the two main conditions for a productive fourth figure: either both premises are affirmative, or they differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) while one premise is universal. There are eight productive moods, obtained through contraposition (al-khalf) or reversal of order, or by conversion of premises. A table is provided showing the major premises (the four types) across the top, the minor premises down the side, and the resulting conclusions. For example, when the major is 'Every B is J' and the minor is 'Every A is B' produces 'Some J is A'? Wait, the table seems to show that from 'Every B is J' and 'Every A is B' we get 'Some J is A' and 'Some J is not A', which is odd. Actually, the fourth figure typically has the middle term as predicate in the major and subject in the minor. Here the author uses A and B and J; careful: the table shows the minor premise as about A and B, and the major as about B and J, so the middle term is B. The conclusions involve A and J. The results shown in the table are not all valid; the table lists both valid and invalid moods. The instruction says 'الساقطات والباقيات' (invalid and valid moods) are clear from the table. The teacher can explain that the fourth figure has limited utility and many of its moods are invalid or require special conditions. All examples use the logical variables A, B, J, consistent with the Islamic logical tradition.

Third Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author presents a visual table summarizing the third figure moods and their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of the third figure and derive the four productive moods from a universal minor.

Core Idea
The third figure requires a universal minor and affirmative major; it always yields a particular conclusion.

We now examine the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The author presents a table summarizing its moods. Understanding this table requires grasping the condition that the minor premise must be universal and the major premise affirmative, and that all conclusions are particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find the line where the author states the condition for the third figure. What must the major and minor premises be?
The text says: 'الكبرى موجبة ويكون الصغرى كلية'.
2
List the possible major premises
The author lists four types of major premises. Write them down: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
3
List the possible minor premises
The table includes four minors: every B is J, no B is J, some B is J, some B is not J. Note that the condition requires the minor to be universal, but the list includes particulars.
This indicates that only the universal minors are used in the productive moods shown.
4
Examine the examples
Each example uses the universal affirmative minor 'كل ب ج' with a different major. Observe the conclusion in each case: it is always particular.
For instance, from 'كل ب أ' and 'كل ب ج' we get 'بعض ج أ'.
5
Understand the pattern
The third figure with a universal minor and any major (affirmative or negative, universal or particular) yields a particular conclusion. This is because the middle term is subject in both premises, forcing a particular conclusion.
This is summarized in the table's four moods.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وهذا مرآة الشكل الثالث. وذلك حيث الكبرى موجبة ويكون الصغرى كلية.

الأمثلة:
- كل ب أ (موجبة كلية) مع كل ب ج ينتج: بعض ج أ.
- لا شيء من ب أ (سالبة كلية) مع كل ب ج ينتج: بعض ج ليس أ.
- بعض ب أ (موجبة جزئية) مع كل ب ج ينتج: بعض ج أ.
- بعض ب ليس أ (سالبة جزئية) مع كل ب ج ينتج: بعض ج ليس أ.
Table of the third figure: And this is the mirror of the third figure. That is where the major premise is affirmative and the minor premise is universal. Major premises: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Minor premises: every B is J, no B is J, some B is J, some B is not J. Examples: Every B is A (universal affirmative) with every B is J produces: some J is A. No B is A (universal negative) with every B is J produces: some J is not A. Some B is A (particular affirmative) with every B is J produces: some J is A. Some B is not A (particular negative) with every B is J produces: some J is not A.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage provides a concise table summarizing the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The third figure is characterized by the middle term being the subject of both premises. The key condition, stated at the outset, is that the major premise must be affirmative and the minor premise must be universal. The table lists the four possible major premises (universal affirmative, etc.) but the minor premises are all universal affirmative? Actually the minor premises shown are 'كل ب ج' (every B is J) which is universal, but then it also lists 'لا شيء من ب ج' (no B is J) which is also universal, and 'بعض ب ج' (some B is J) which is particular, and 'بعض ب ليس ج' (some B is not J) which is particular negative. This is confusing. The condition says 'الصغرى كلية' but the table includes particulars. Possibly the table is showing combinations where the minor is taken from the set of universal minors? The examples all have the minor as 'كل ب ج' (universal affirmative), but the table lists four minors. The text likely has a mis-OCR. We keep the Arabic as is, but note that the standard third figure requires the minor to be affirmative and universal. The teacher can explain that the table is illustrating only the productive moods when the minor is universal. The examples show that from a universal affirmative minor (every B is J) and various majors, we get particular conclusions. For instance, from 'Every B is A' and 'Every B is J', we conclude 'Some J is A' (by Darapti). This is a classic third figure mood. All Islamic logic examples use such notation.
