Refining the Rule: The Conclusion Does Not Always Follow the Inferior Premise
Author draws a rule about the conclusion following the inferior premise, then corrects it with Ibn Sina's verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Sina's refined rule to determine the quality and quantity of a categorical syllogism's conclusion and identify the two exceptions.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's verification shows the conclusion's quantity follows the minor and its quality follows the major, with two exceptions.

You might have learned a simple rule: the conclusion in a syllogism follows the 'inferior' premise—negative in quality, particular in quantity. But Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in al-Isharat offers a more precise analysis. He assigns different roles to the minor and major premises, and he identifies two exceptional cases where the rule shifts. Mastering this refinement will deepen your understanding of syllogistic validity, especially in modal contexts common in kalam.

Guided Path
1
State the common rule
The common rule says the conclusion follows the inferior premise: if one premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if one is particular, the conclusion is particular. Identify the inferior in quality (negative) and quantity (particular).
This rule is a helpful shortcut but not always accurate.
2
Distinguish quality and quantity
Quality is whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. Quantity is whether it is universal or particular. The 'inferior' in quality is the negative; in quantity, the particular.
3
Present Ibn Sina's correction
Ibn Sina states that the conclusion follows the minor premise in quantity and the major premise in quality and modality. So quantity depends on the minor, not simply the inferior overall.
Modality (jiha) refers to necessity, possibility, or absoluteness.
4
Note the two exceptions
First: if the minor is possible and the major is not necessary, the conclusion follows the minor in modality (not the major). Second: if the minor is affirmative necessary and the major is absolute, then if the major is universal, the conclusion is like the minor (affirmative necessary); if the major is particular, the premises are contradictory and no syllogism results.
These exceptions show the depth of modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة: ولعلك علمت مما ألقينا عليك أن النتيجة في القياس تتبع أدون المقدمتين في الكيف والكم. والأدون في الكيف هو السلب، وفي الكم هو الجزئية؛ فالقياس المركب من موجبة وسالبة ينتج سالبة والمركب من كلية وجزئية إنما ينتج جزئية، وأما المركب من الكليتين فربما ينتج كلية، وقد ينتج جزئية.
تتبع أدون المقدمتين إلخ: اعلم أن المنطقيين ذهبوا إلى أن النتيجة تتبع أخس المقدمتين كما ذكر المصنف. وحقق الشيخ في الإشارات أنه ليس كذلك مطلقا، بل هي تابعة في الكمية للصغرى، وفي الكيفية والجهة للكبرى، إلا في موضعين: أحدهما أن يكون الصغرى ممكنة والكبرى غير ضرورية؛ فإن النتيجة تكون في الفعل والقوة تابعة للصغرى لا للكبرى. والثاني: أن يكون الصغرى موجبة ضرورية والكبرى مطلقا؛ فإنها وإن كانت عامة أنتجت كالصغرى موجبة ضرورية، وإن كانت خاصة لم يكن الاقتران قياسا؛ لتناقض المقدمتين.
Benefit: Perhaps you have learned from what we have presented that the conclusion in a syllogism follows the inferior of the two premises in quality and quantity. The inferior in quality is negation, and in quantity is particularity; so the syllogism composed of an affirmative and a negative produces a negative, and the syllogism composed of a universal and a particular yields a particular; as for the syllogism composed of two universals, it sometimes yields a universal and sometimes yields a particular. 'The conclusion follows the inferior premise, etc.:' Know that the logicians held that the conclusion follows the most inferior of the two premises as the author mentioned. But the Shaykh in al-Isharat verified that this is not absolutely the case; rather, it follows the minor in quantity, and the major in quality and modality, except in two cases: one is when the minor is possible and the major is not necessary; then the conclusion in actuality and potentiality follows the minor, not the major. The second: when the minor is affirmative necessary and the major is absolute; then if the major is general, the conclusion is like the minor, affirmative necessary, and if it is specific, the connection is not a syllogism due to the contradiction of the premises.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage first states a common rule: the conclusion in a categorical syllogism follows the 'inferior' premise, meaning the one that is either negative (inferior in quality) or particular (inferior in quantity). For example, if one premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. If both are universal, the conclusion may be universal or particular. Then the author provides a critical refinement from Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) in al-Isharat. Ibn Sina says the rule is not absolute. Instead, the conclusion follows the minor premise in quantity, and the major premise in quality and modality. However, there are two exceptions. First, if the minor is possible and the major is not necessary, the conclusion follows the minor in modality. Second, if the minor is affirmative necessary and the major is absolute (non-modal), then if the major is universal (general), the conclusion is like the minor (affirmative necessary); if the major is particular, then the combination is not a valid syllogism due to contradiction. This lesson refines a common generalization, showing the depth of the tradition. The teacher should emphasize that the verification from Ibn Sina is a hallmark of Islamic logic's critical approach. The use of modal terms (necessary, possible, absolute) is typical in discussions of modality. Examples from kalam: 'Every human is necessarily a body' (necessary), 'Some white thing is possibly black' (possible).

Conditional Categorical Syllogisms: Structure and Types
Author introduces conditional categorical syllogisms, gives an example, and divides them into five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term in a conditional categorical syllogism and classify it into one of the five divisions.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms follow the same rules as assertoric ones, but with if-then or either-or premises.

In this passage, the author extends the theory of categorical syllogisms from assertoric premises to conditional ones. The same four figures and conditions apply, as seen in the example: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is animal; whenever he is animal, he is body; therefore whenever Zayd is human, he is body.' The author also lists five types of conditional syllogisms based on the combination of premise kinds. We will walk through these concepts step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy to assertoric syllogisms
The author states that conditional syllogisms have the same figures, moods, and conditions as assertoric ones. This means if you understand the categorical Barbara mood, you already understand its conditional counterpart.
The example uses all universal affirmative conditionals, analogous to Barbara.
2
Identify the middle term in a conditional syllogism
The middle term is the part that appears in both premises—either the antecedent (muqaddam) or consequent (tali). The author maps the four figures by the position of the middle term: e.g., first figure when middle term is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major.
In the example, the middle term is 'animal'.
3
Analyze the example in the first figure
The syllogism: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is animal; whenever he is animal, he is body; therefore whenever Zayd is human, he is body.' The middle term 'animal' appears as consequent in the first premise and antecedent in the second, confirming first figure.
This mirrors the assertoric: 'Every human is animal, every animal is body, therefore every human is body.'
4
Enumerate the five divisions of conditional syllogisms
The author lists five types based on premise composition: (1) two conjunctives, (2) two disjunctives, (3) conjunctive + assertoric, (4) disjunctive + assertoric, (5) conjunctive + disjunctive. The first type is the most important.
The example belongs to the first type.
5
Note the omission of the fourth figure
The author does not discuss the fourth figure for the first division due to lack of utility, directing interested students to advanced works like Sharh al-Matali'.
This is a practical pedagogical decision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاقترانيات من الشرطيات وحالها في الأشكال الأربعة، والضروب المنتجة، والشرائط المعتبرة كحال الاقترانيات من الحمليات سواء بسواء. مثال الشكل الأول في المتصلة "كلما كان زيد إنسانا كان حيوانا، وكلما كان حيوانا كان جسما" ينتج "كلما كان زيد إنسانا كان جسما".
فصل في الاقترانيات إلخ: اعلم أن الحمليات كما تنقسم إلى بديهيات ونظريات محتاجة إلى الحجة، كذلك الشرطيات قد تكون بديهية كقولنا: "كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود". وقد تكون نظرية كقولنا: "متى وجدت الحركة المستقيمة وجد محدد الجهات، ومتى وجد الممكن وجد الواجب" فمست الحاجة إلى معرفة الأقيسة الشرطية الاقترانية. وينعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعة؛ لأن الحد الأوسط إما أن يكون تاليا في الصغرى، ومقدما في الكبرى فهو الأول، أو بالعكس فهو الرابع، أو تاليا فيهما فهو الثاني، وإن كان مقدما فيهما فهو الثالث. مثال الشكل الأول إلخ: اعلم أن القياس الاقتراني الشرطي على خمسة أقسام: الأول: ما يتركب من متصلتين. الثاني: ما يتركب من منفصلتين. الثالث: ما يتركب من متصلة وحملية. الرابع: ما يتركب من منفصلة وحملية. الخامس: ما يتركب من متصلة ومنفصلة. والعمدة من هذه الأقسام ما يتركب من متصلتين، فقوله: "مثال الشكل الأول إلخ" أي من القسم الأول الذي يتركب من متصلتين. ولم يتعرض للشكل الرابع لهذا القسم لقلة المنفعة. فإن شئت تفصيل الضروب وبيان إنتاجها فانظر في شرح المطالع وغيره من المبسوطات.
Chapter on categorical syllogisms from conditionals, and their situation in the four figures, and the productive moods, and the considered conditions are exactly like the situation of categorical syllogisms from assertoric premises. Example of the first figure in conjunctive conditionals: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is animal; and whenever he is animal, he is body' yields 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is body.' 'Chapter on categorical syllogisms from conditionals, etc.': Know that just as assertoric premises are divided into axiomatic and theoretical needing proof, likewise conditionals can be axiomatic, like our saying: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' And they can be theoretical, like our saying: 'Whenever rectilinear motion exists, the determination of directions exists, and whenever the contingent exists, the Necessary exists.' So the need arises to know conditional categorical syllogisms. And the four figures are realized in them because the middle term is either consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major — that is the first; or the opposite — that is the fourth; or consequent in both — that is the second; or antecedent in both — that is the third. 'Example of the first figure, etc.': Know that the conditional categorical syllogism is of five divisions: first: composed of two conjunctive conditionals. Second: composed of two disjunctive conditionals. Third: composed of a conjunctive conditional and an assertoric. Fourth: composed of a disjunctive conditional and an assertoric. Fifth: composed of a conjunctive and a disjunctive conditional. The most important of these divisions is that composed of two conjunctives. So his saying: 'Example of the first figure, etc.' means from the first division, which is composed of two conjunctives. And he did not address the fourth figure for this division due to lack of utility. If you want the details of the moods and their production, look in Sharh al-Matali' and other extensive works.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage moves from categorical (assertoric) syllogisms to conditional categorical syllogisms (iqtiraniyyat shartiyya). The author first states that conditional syllogisms share the same four figures and conditions as assertoric syllogisms. An example is given in the first figure using conjunctive conditionals (muttasila): 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is animal; whenever he is animal, he is body' yields 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is body.' This mirrors the categorical Barbara mood. The commentary then explains that conditionals can be axiomatic (like 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists') or theoretical (like 'Whenever rectilinear motion exists, directions are determined; whenever the contingent exists, the Necessary exists'), requiring the study of conditional logic. The middle term in conditional syllogisms is identified by its position as antecedent or consequent in the premises. The author then divides conditional categorical syllogisms into five types based on the kinds of premises: (1) two conjunctive conditionals, (2) two disjunctive conditionals, (3) a conjunctive conditional and an assertoric, (4) a disjunctive conditional and an assertoric, and (5) a conjunctive and a disjunctive conditional. The most important is the first type (two conjunctives), which is what the example illustrates. The author notes that the fourth figure for this type is omitted due to low utility, referring interested readers to larger works. This lesson shows the expansion of syllogistic theory to conditional propositions, a hallmark of Islamic logic. The teacher can use the example: 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists; whenever daytime exists, we can see; therefore whenever the sun rises, we can see' (though this is not from the text).

Definition and Divisions of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author defines conditional conjunctive syllogisms and enumerates five divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five divisions of conditional conjunctive syllogisms and explain how the four figures are determined based on the middle term.

Core Idea
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms mirror categorical syllogisms in figures and moods.

Just as categorical syllogisms can be self-evident or theoretical, so too conditional conjunctive syllogisms follow the same logical structure. In this lesson, we explore the definition of these syllogisms, the five types based on the composition of premises, and the four figures determined by the position of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
The author explicitly states that conditional conjunctive syllogisms have the same four figures, productive moods, and conditions as categorical syllogisms.
2
Distinguish self-evident from theoretical
Conditionals can be self-evident (e.g., 'If the sun is shining, daytime exists') or theoretical (e.g., 'If rectilinear motion exists, the limit of directions exists'). This shows that conditionals require proof just like categoricals.
3
Understand the four figures
The four figures are determined by the position of the middle term: first figure (middle term is consequent in minor, antecedent in major), second (consequent in both), third (antecedent in both), fourth (antecedent in minor, consequent in major).
The fourth figure is omitted in the first division due to lack of benefit.
4
Enumerate the five divisions
The five divisions are: 1) composed of two conditionals (most important), 2) two disjunctives, 3) a conditional and a categorical, 4) a disjunctive and a categorical (text likely missing 'disjunctive'), 5) a conditional and a disjunctive.
The first division is the most important as it is the purest form.
5
Note coverage in the compendium
The author only covers the first division (three figures) and part of the second and third, leaving the rest for advanced works like Sharh al-Mutali'.

Section on conditional conjunctive syllogisms and their state in the four figures, the productive moods, and the conditions considered, exactly like the state of categorical conjunctive syllogisms. 'Section on conditional conjunctives, etc.': Know that just as categoricals are divided into self-evident and theoretical needing proof, so conditionals may be self-evident like our saying 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists.' And they may be theoretical like our saying 'When rectilinear motion exists, the limit of directions exists; and when the contingent exists, the Necessary exists.' So his saying 'Example of the first figure, etc.' means from the first division which is composed of two conditionals. And he did not address the fourth figure for this division due to lack of benefit. If you wish the detail of the moods and their productivity, then look in Sharh al-Mutali' and other extended works. Example of the second figure: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal; and not at all whenever he is a stone, he is an animal' yields 'Not at all if Zayd is human, he is a stone.' Example of the third figure: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal; and whenever Zayd is human, he is a writer' yields 'It may be that whenever Zayd is an animal, he is a writer.' As for the conditional conjunctive composed of disjunctives, its example from the first figure: 'Either every A is B, or every C is D; and always every D is E, or every F is G' yields 'Always either every A is B, or every C is G, or every F is G.' As for the conditional conjunctive composed of a categorical and a conditional, like our saying: 'Whenever B is C, then every C is A; and every A is D' yields 'Whenever B is C, then every J is A.' And analogously for the remaining compositions. 'The remaining compositions, etc.': You have known that the conditional syllogism is of five divisions, and in each division the four figures are formed. The author ﷲ sufficed in the first division with three figures, and in the second and third divisions with the first figure, and left the fourth and fifth divisions entirely, relying on the student's mind, and because this compendium cannot bear their moods and results. If you wish to recall and master them, then you must refer to Sharh al-Mutali' and Sharh al-Qutbiyya by al-Qutb al-Razi.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (الاقترانيات من الشرطيات). The author first states that these syllogisms follow the same four figures, moods, and conditions as categorical conjunctive syllogisms. An example of the first figure with two conditionals is given: if Zayd is human then animal, and if animal then body, therefore if human then body. The commentary then explains that conditionals, like categoricals, can be self-evident or theoretical. Examples and Figures in Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author gives examples of each figure using conditional premises and symbolic variables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a conditional conjunctive syllogism in the first figure and identify the figure of a given example.

Core Idea
The middle term's position as antecedent or consequent determines the figure, just like in categorical syllogisms.

The author provides concrete examples of conditional conjunctive syllogisms in each figure, using both linguistic examples and symbolic variables. In this lesson, we will analyse these examples to understand how the middle term determines the figure and how to verify the validity of the syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first figure example
The example: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal; whenever he is an animal, he is a body; therefore whenever Zayd is human, he is a body.' The middle term is 'animal' (consequent of first, antecedent of second). This fits the first figure.
2
Examine the second figure example
The example: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal; and not at all whenever he is a stone, he is an animal; therefore not at all if Zayd is human, he is a stone.' The middle term 'animal' is the consequent in both premises. This is the second figure.
The second figure requires a negative premise to be productive.
3
Examine the third figure example
The example: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal; and whenever Zayd is human, he is a writer; therefore it may be that whenever Zayd is an animal, he is a writer.' The middle term 'human' is the antecedent in both premises. This is the third figure.
The conclusion is particular, not universal, due to the third figure's nature.
4
Understand the symbolic examples
The author gives a disjunctive example using variables: 'Either every A is B, or every C is D; and always every D is E, or every F is G; yields always either every A is B, or every C is G, or every F is G.' This shows the pattern for composite syllogisms.
These symbolic forms help in generalizing the structure.
5
Recognize the author's selectivity
The author only covers three figures of the first division and the first figure of the second and third divisions, leaving the rest for advanced texts.
This is due to the compendium's brevity; full details are in Sharh al-Mutali'.

The Syllogism

The Exceptional Syllogism: Modus Ponens and Modus Tollens in Islamic Logic
Author defines exceptional syllogism and explains the two valid rules of inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid inference rules of an exceptional syllogism and apply them to conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Exceptional syllogism: one-way entailment from antecedent to consequent defines two valid forms.

The exceptional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) is a second major type of syllogism in Islamic logic. Unlike the conjunctive syllogism that links categorical propositions, the exceptional syllogism combines a conditional premise (if-then) with a categorical premise that makes an exception (affirming or denying one part). The author explains that only two forms are valid: affirming the antecedent to conclude the consequent, and denying the consequent to conclude the denial of the antecedent. Understanding these forms is crucial for evaluating arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh that use conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Look for a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) and a categorical premise containing an exception word (istithnā'). The conditional establishes a necessary relation between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī).
2
Distinguish the two valid forms
The author states: (1) affirming the antecedent yields the consequent; (2) denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent. Note that the reverse forms are invalid: affirming the consequent does not yield the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not yield the denial of the consequent.
These correspond to modus ponens and modus tollens in Western logic.
3
Understand the logical basis
The antecedent is the 'necessary entailer' (malzūm) and the consequent is the 'necessary' (lāzim). The existence of the malzūm guarantees the lāzim, but not vice versa because the lāzim may be more general. Absence of the lāzim guarantees absence of the malzūm, but not vice versa.
This one-way entailment is why only two forms are valid.
4
Apply the example
Use the author's example: 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists.' Test both valid forms: affirming the sun is shining gives daytime; denying daytime gives sun not shining. Test the invalid forms: affirming daytime does not necessarily give sun shining (daytime could have other causes), and denying sun shining does not necessarily give no daytime (daytime could be from other causes).
5
Check for the common error
Many mistakenly think denying the antecedent is also valid. The source explicitly warns against this. Always check: only modus ponens (affirming antecedent) and modus tollens (denying consequent) are valid.
Fallacious forms are called 'fallacy of denying the antecedent' and 'fallacy of affirming the consequent'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاستثنائي: وهو مركب من مقدمتين أي قضيتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى حملية، ويتخلل بينهما كلمة الاستثناء - أعني إلا وأخواتها - ومن ثَمّ يسمّى استثنائيا. فإن كانت الشرطية متصلة فاستثناء عين المقدّم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج رفع المقدّم كما تقول : "كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا" القياس الاستثنائي إلخ قد سلف أن القياس قسمان: اقتراني واستثنائي. وإذ قد فرغ عن الاقتراني وأقسامه وأحكامه شرع في الاستثنائي، وهو مركب من مقدمتين. فاستثناء عين المقدَّم إلخ: لأن وجود الملزوم مستلزم اللازم و لا عكس؛ الجواز كون اللازم أعم؛ فلا يلزم من وضعه وضعه. واستثناء نقيض التالي إلخ: لاستلزام عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم، فرفعه رفعه ولا عکس؛ الجواز أن يكون اللازم أعم.
Section on the exceptional syllogism: It is composed of two premises, i.e., two propositions: one conditional and the other categorical, and between them intervenes the word of exception - I mean 'except' and its sisters - and therefore it is called exceptional. If the conditional is a conditional, then the exception of the very antecedent yields the very consequent, and the exception of the contradictory of the consequent yields the raising of the antecedent, as you say: 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists.' The exceptional syllogism, etc.: It has preceded that the syllogism is two divisions: conjunctive and exceptional. And since he has finished with the conjunctive and its divisions and rules, he begins the exceptional. And it is composed of two premises. 'So exception of the very antecedent, etc.': Because the existence of the necessary entailer necessitates the necessary, but not its converse, because the necessary may be more general; so its positing does not necessitate its positing. And 'exception of the contradictory of the consequent, etc.': Because the absence of the necessary necessitates the absence of the necessary entailer, so raising it raises it, and no converse, because the necessary may be more general.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the exceptional syllogism (القياس الاستثنائي). It is defined as a syllogism composed of two premises: one conditional and one categorical, with the word 'except' or similar (the exception) inserted between them. The commentator notes that the syllogism is divided into conjunctive and exceptional, and now the exceptional is treated. Two valid forms are given: 1) If the conditional is of the type 'if...then...', then affirming the antecedent (i.e., stating it as true) yields the consequent; 2) Denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. The commentary explains the logic: the antecedent is the necessary entailer (ملزوم) of the consequent (لازم), so affirming the antecedent entails the consequent; but the converse is not always true because the consequent may be more general. Similarly, denying the consequent denies the antecedent because the absence of the necessary necessitates absence of the necessary entailer. The example uses the sun and daytime: if the sun is shining, daytime exists. Affirming that the sun is shining gives daytime; denying that daytime exists gives that the sun is not shining. The commentary emphasizes the one-way entailment.

الشرطية
المقدّم
التالي
الاستثناء
الملزوم واللازم

Key Takeaway
The exceptional syllogism has two and only two valid inference patterns: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens).

Figures in Conditional Syllogisms (Muttasila)
Examples of second and third figures of conditional conjunctive syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a conditional conjunctive syllogism composed of two conditionals by locating the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms have figures just like categorical ones.

This passage presents examples of conditional syllogisms in the second and third figures. Understanding how figures are determined by the middle term's position is crucial for evaluating their validity. We will analyze the examples to see how the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises and the conclusion
For the second figure example: Premise1: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal'; Premise2: 'It is never the case that if he is a stone, he is an animal'; Conclusion: 'It is never the case that if Zayd is a human, he is a stone'. For the third figure example: Premise1: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal'; Premise2: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is a writer'; Conclusion: 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a writer'.
2
Find the middle term common to both premises
In the second figure, the common term is 'animal', which appears as the consequent of both conditionals (in premise1 it's the consequent; in premise2 it's the consequent after negation). In the third figure, the common term is 'Zayd is a human', which is the antecedent of both conditionals.
3
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
Is the middle term the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali) in each premise? In the second figure, it is the consequent in both premises. In the third figure, it is the antecedent in both premises.
The first figure would have the middle term as antecedent in the first premise and consequent in the second.
4
Match the pattern to the figure definition
In conditional syllogisms, the figure is defined by the position of the middle term. Second figure: consequent in both premises. Third figure: antecedent in both premises. Thus the examples correspond to those figures.
The author only gave examples for the second and third figures of this type, leaving the first figure to be worked out by analogy.

باقي التركيبات إلخ قد عرفت أن القياس الشرطي على خمسة أقسام، وفي كل قسم ينعقد الأربعة. واكتفى المصنف الله في القسم الأول على ثلاثة أشكال وفي القسم الثاني والثالث على الشكل الأول، وترك القسم الرابع والخامس؛ رأساً اعتماداً على ذهن المتعلم، ولأن هذا المختصر لا يتحمل ضروبها ونتائجها. فإن شئت الاستحضار والضبط فعليك بشرح "المطالع و شرح القطبية للقطب الرازي".

The exceptive syllogism: example of the second figure: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal; and it is never the case that if he is a stone, he is an animal' produces 'It is never the case that if Zayd is a human, he is a stone'. Example of the third: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal; and whenever Zayd is a human, he is a writer' produces 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a writer'. As for the conditional conjunctive composed of disjunctives, its example from the first figure: 'Either every A is B, or every J is D; and always every D is H, or every D is Z' produces 'Always either every A is B, or every J is H, or every D is Z'. As for the conditional conjunctive composed of a categorical and a conditional, like our statement: 'Whenever B is J, then every J is A; and every H is A' produces 'Whenever B is J, then every J is A'. And on this analogy are the remaining compositions. 'The remaining compositions etc.': You have known that the conditional syllogism has five divisions, and in each division four figures occur. The author (may Allah [protect him]) sufficed in the first division with three figures, and in the second and third divisions with the first figure, and left the fourth and fifth divisions entirely, relying on the student's mind, and because this abridgment cannot bear their moods and conclusions. If you wish to recall and master them, then you must consult the commentary of al-Mutala' and the commentary of al-Qutbiyya by al-Qutb al-Razi.

The Syllogism
advanced

Disjunctive Conditional Syllogisms: The First Figure
Example of a disjunctive conditional syllogism in the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a disjunctive conditional syllogism in the first figure and identify the components: premises, middle term, and conclusion.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditional syllogisms chain disjunctions through a common middle term.

The passage then presents an example of a conditional conjunctive syllogism composed of disjunctives (munfasila) in the first figure. This is a complex form that uses a middle term appearing in both premises to connect and form a new disjunction. We will walk through the example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Premise 1: 'Either every A is B, or every J is D'. Premise 2: 'Always either every D is H, or every D is Z'.
The variables are Arabic letters used as placeholders.
2
Find the middle term that appears in both premises
The term D appears in both premises: in Premise 1 as the consequent of the second disjunct, and in Premise 2 as the subject of both disjuncts.
3
Recognize the figure
The author states this is an example of the first figure. In disjunctive conditional syllogisms, the first figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate of the first premise and the subject of the second premise (or similar). Here D is predicate in first premise and subject in second, fitting the first figure pattern.
The exact definition of figures for disjunctive syllogisms may vary, but the author assigns it to the first figure.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a new disjunction that combines the non-middle terms: 'Always either every A is B, or every J is H, or every D is Z'. Notice that D disappears as a link, and the alternatives from both premises are merged.
The conclusion has three parts: the first disjunct from Premise 1 (A is B), the second disjunct from Premise 2 (D is Z), and a new combination (J is H) which comes from replacing D in the first premise's second disjunct with H (the alternative from the second premise).

Mixed Conditional Syllogisms and the Author's Selective Coverage
Commentary on the classification and pedagogical method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the five types of conditional syllogisms and explain the pedagogical reason for the author's selective coverage.

Core Idea
The author's brevity is a deliberate teaching method: the student must complete the patterns through independent study.

After presenting examples of purely conditional and disjunctive syllogisms, the author briefly mentions mixed syllogisms combining a categorical and a conditional, then comments on the overall classification and the reason for not covering all possibilities. This lesson clarifies the five divisions and the author's pedagogical strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed syllogism example
The author gives: 'Whenever B is J, then every J is A; and every H is A' and says it produces 'Whenever B is J, then every J is A'. This appears trivial (the conclusion repeats one premise), indicating that this may be a placeholder example; the actual pattern is left to the student.
The author may be showing that the conclusion follows by substitution, but the exact reasoning is condensed.
2
Learn the five divisions of conditional syllogisms
The commentary explains: the conditional syllogism has five divisions: (1) composed of two conditionals (mutasila), (2) composed of two disjunctives (munfasila), (3) composed of one conditional and one disjunctive, (4) composed of a categorical and a conditional, (5) composed of a categorical and a disjunctive. The author covered (1) with three figures, (2) and (3) with only the first figure, and omitted (4) and (5) entirely.
These divisions are standard in the logical tradition.
3
Understand the author's pedagogical method
The author openly states that he omitted the fourth and fifth divisions 'relying on the student's mind' and because the abridgment cannot bear the full details. This is a common approach in mukhtasar works: the teacher provides enough to guide the student, who must complete the rest through analogy and consultation of longer commentaries.
The author recommends al-Mutala' and al-Qutbiyya for complete coverage.

The Syllogism

Rules of Exceptive Conditional Syllogisms
Author defines exceptive syllogism and states its rules for muttasila and munfasila with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid inference forms for conjunctive exceptive syllogisms and for each type of disjunctive exceptive syllogism (real, man'at al-jam', man'at al-khuluw).

Core Idea
The validity of an exceptive syllogism depends on the logical relationship between the parts of the conditional.

In this lesson, we explore the exceptive syllogism, a fundamental tool in Islamic logic. You will learn how to reason from a conditional premise and a categorical exception. We will cover both conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or) conditionals, with special attention to the different types of disjunctives and their valid inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional premise
Look for particles like 'كلما' (whenever) for conjunctive, or 'إما' (either) for disjunctive. This determines which rules apply.
Conjunctive conditionals imply a necessary connection; disjunctives involve mutual exclusion.
2
For conjunctive: determine the categorical premise
Check whether the categorical premise affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent. Only these two forms yield valid conclusions (modus ponens and modus tollens).
Affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent are fallacies.
3
For disjunctive: classify the disjunction
Determine if the disjunction is real (haqiqiyya), man'at al-jam' (incompatibility in truth), or man'at al-khuluw (incompatibility in falsehood) based on whether the parts can be true/false together.
The classification is given in the commentary using examples like even/odd (real) and tree/stone (man'at al-jam').
4
Apply the rule for the disjunction type
For real disjunction: affirming one and denying one both yield the opposite. For man'at al-jam': only affirming one yields negation of the other. For man'at al-khuluw: only denying one yields affirmation of the other.
Memorize the patterns: real = both, jam' = only affirmation, khuluw = only denial.
5
Practice with examples
Use the examples in the passage (sun/daytime, even/odd, tree/stone) to test your understanding. Identify the type and check the validity of the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاستثنائي: وهو مركب من مقدمتين أي قضيتين: إحداهما شرطية والأخرى حملية، ويتخلل بينهما كلمة الاستثناء - أعني إلا وأخواتها - ومن ثَمّ يسمّى استثنائيا. فإن كانت الشرطية متصلة فاستثناء عين المقدّم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج رفع المقدّم كما تقول : "كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا لكن الشمس طالعة " . ينتج "فالنهار موجود" ، "لكن النهار ليس موجود" ينتج "فالشمس ليست بطالعة. وإن كانت منفصلة حقيقية فاستثناء عين أحدهما ينتج نقيض الآخر وبالعكس. وفي مانعة الجمع ينتج القسم الأول دون الثاني. وفي مانعة الخلو القسم الثاني دون الأول.

القياس الاستثنائي إلخ قد سلف أن القياس قسمان: اقتراني واستثنائي. وإذ قد فرغ عن الاقتراني وأقسامه وأحكامه شرع في الاستثنائي، وهو مركب من مقدمتين. فاستثناء عين المقدَّم إلخ: لأن وجود الملزوم مستلزم اللازم و لا عكس؛ الجواز كون اللازم أعم؛ فلا يلزم من وضعه وضعه. واستثناء نقيض التالي إلخ: لاستلزام عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم، فرفعه رفعه ولا عکس؛ الجواز أن يكون اللازم أعم.

المنفصلة فاستثناء عين أحدهما إلخ : اعلم أنه إذا كان المنافاة بين المقدّم والتالي في الصدق والكذب معا كما الحقيقية، فينتج وضع كل رفع الآخر ، ورفع كل وضع الآخر، لامتناع الاجتماع والارتفاع، فيحصل نتائج أربعة كقولنا : العدد إما زوج أو فرد لكنه زوج ينتج أنه ليس بفرد، لكنه فرد فليس بزوج، لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد، لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج. وإن كان المنافاة في الصدق فقط، فينتج وضع كل رفع الآخر، وإلا لزم صدقهما ولا عکس؛ الجواز ارتفاعهما :نحو هذا الشيء إما شجر أو حجر، فإذا كان شجرا لم يكن حجرا، وإذا كان حجرا لم يكن شجرا. وإن كان المنافاة في الكذب فقط ينتج رفع كل وضع الآخر؛ وإلا يلزم كذبهما معا، لا وضع كل رفع الآخر؛ لجواز ارتفاعهما صدقا.

ينتج القسم الأول إلخ أي استثناء عين أي جزء كان ينتج نقيض الآخر؛ لامتناع الجمع بينهما، ولا ينتج استثناء نقيض شيء من جزئيهما عين الآخر؛ لجواز ارتفاعهما. القسم الثاني دون الأول إلخ: أي استثناء نقيض أي جزء كان ينتج عين الآخر لامتناع ارتفاعهما، ولا ينتج استثناء عين شيء من جزئيهما نقيض الآخر لجواز الجمع.

وههنا قد انتهت مباحث القياس بالقول المجمل والتفصيل موكول إلى الكتب الطوال.
Chapter on the exceptive syllogism: It is composed of two premises, i.e., two propositions: one conditional, the other categorical; and the word of exception interposes between them – I mean 'except' and its sisters – and therefore it is called exceptive. If the conditional is a conjunctive (muttasila), then excepting the very antecedent produces the very consequent, and excepting the contradictory of the consequent produces the negation of the antecedent, as you say: 'Whenever the sun is risen, daytime exists; but the sun is risen' produces 'So daytime exists'; 'but daytime does not exist' produces 'So the sun is not risen.' If it is a real disjunctive, then excepting one of the two produces the contradictory of the other and vice versa. In the case of man'at al-jam' (incompatibility), the first part [excepting a part] produces the negation of the other, but not the second. In the case of man'at al-khuluw (incompatibility in absence), the second part [excepting the negation] produces the other, but not the first. 'The exceptive syllogism etc.': It has preceded that the syllogism is two divisions: conjunctive and exceptive. Now that he has finished with the conjunctive and its divisions and rules, he begins with the exceptive, and it is composed of two premises. 'So excepting the very antecedent etc.' because the existence of the conditional entails the consequent and not conversely; it is possible that the consequent is more general; so its existence does not entail the antecedent's existence. 'And excepting the contradictory of the consequent etc.' because the non-existence of the consequent entails the non-existence of the antecedent, so denying it denies it and not conversely; it is possible that the consequent is more general. 'The disjunctive: excepting one of them etc.': Know that if the opposition between antecedent and consequent is in both truth and falsehood, as in the real [disjunctive], then positing each negates the other, and negating each posits the other, because they cannot coexist nor both be absent; thus four results obtain, like our saying: 'The number is either even or odd; but it is even' produces 'it is not odd'; 'but it is odd' produces 'it is not even'; 'but it is not even' produces 'it is odd'; 'but it is not odd' produces 'it is even.' If the opposition is only in truth, then positing each negates the other, otherwise they would both be true, and not conversely; it is possible both are false: like 'This thing is either a tree or a stone'; so if it is a tree, it is not a stone; if it is a stone, it is not a tree. If the opposition is only in falsehood, then negating each posits the other, otherwise they would both be false; not positing each negates the other, because it is possible they are both true. 'The first part produces without the second etc.' means excepting the very of whichever part it may be produces the contradictory of the other, because they cannot both be true; and excepting the contradictory of one of its parts does not produce the other because it is possible both are false. 'The second part without the first etc.' means excepting the contradictory of whichever part it may be produces the other, because they cannot both be false; and excepting the very one of its parts does not produce the contradictory of the other because it is possible both are true. And here the topics of syllogism have ended in summary, and the detail is entrusted to the longer books.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptive syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i) and explains its rules. The author first gives the definition: it consists of a conditional and a categorical premise, with an exceptive particle. For a conditional proposition (muttasila), affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. An example is given using the sun and daytime. For disjunctives, the rules depend on the type of opposition. The commentary clarifies that in a real disjunctive (haqiqiyya), where the two parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive, affirming one denies the other and vice versa, yielding four valid inferences. For man'at al-jam' (incompatibility in truth), affirming one denies the other, but denying one does not affirm the other. For man'at al-khuluw (incompatibility in falsehood), denying one affirms the other, but affirming one does not deny the other. The commentary further explains the rationale using Islamic examples like the parity of numbers and types of objects.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
استثناء عين المقدّم
استثناء نقيض التالي
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what does 'but daytime does not exist' produce when combined with 'Whenever the sun is risen, daytime exists'?
A) Daytime exists
B) The sun is not risen
C) The sun is risen
D) No valid conclusion

Key Takeaway
The exceptive syllogism provides two valid patterns for conjunctive conditionals and specific patterns for disjunctives depending on the type of opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conjunctive exceptive syllogism about the permissibility of fasting on a journey: If a person is on a journey, then he is exempted from fasting. Ahmad is on a journey. What is the conclusion? Is it valid?
?
Classify the following disjunctive as real, man'at al-jam', or man'at al-khuluw: 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden.' If you affirm it is obligatory, what can you conclude?

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogisms: Structure and Types
Defines compound syllogisms and their two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify compound syllogisms as mawsul or mafsul and reconstruct a multi-step syllogistic chain.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism chains simple syllogisms back to axiomatic premises.

In earlier lessons, we learned that a single syllogism produces a conclusion from exactly two premises. But many proofs in Islamic sciences require multiple steps. This passage introduces the compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab), a series of syllogisms that terminates at self-evident premises. It also distinguishes two forms: mawsul (where every intermediate conclusion is stated) and mafsul (where some are omitted). Understanding this structure is essential for evaluating long chains of reasoning in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic syllogistic unit
Every syllogism has exactly two premises and one conclusion. Start by isolating the atomic syllogisms in a chain.
2
Recognize the need for chaining
If the desired conclusion cannot be drawn directly from conceded premises, a series of syllogisms is required. Each intermediate conclusion becomes a premise for the next step.
3
Classify as mawsul or mafsul
In mawsul, all intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated. In mafsul, some are omitted but still logically implied. Compare the examples in the passage using letters J, B, A, D, H.
The passage first gives: 'Every J is B, every B is A, so every J is A; and every A is D, so every J is D; and every D is H, so every J is H' – that is mawsul. Then it gives a mafsul version with fewer explicit steps.
4
Trace the chain back to axioms
Ensure the chain ultimately terminates at self-evident (badihi) premises. This guarantees the soundness of the overall argument.

فصل: ومن الأقيسة المركبة قياس يسمّى قياس الخُلف، ومرجعه إلى قياسين: أحدهما: اقتراني شرطي مركب من المتصلتين، وثانيهما: استثنائي إحدى مقدمتيه لزومية. من الأقيسة إلخ: اعلم أن القياس المنتج المطلوب لا يكون مركبا إلا من مقدمتين، لا أزيد ولا أنقص، لكن قد يحتاج في حصول المطلوب إلى كسب قياس آخر كذلك حتى ينتهي الكسب إلى المقدمات البديهية؛ فيكون هناك قياسات مرتبة محصلة للقياس المنتج للمطلوب، ويسمّى قياسا مركبا، وهو قد يكون موصول النتائج؛ بأن يكون جميع نتائج تلك الأقيسة مصرحة كقولنا: "كل" ج ب ، وكل ب أ، فكل ج أ، وكل أد، فكل ج د، وكل د ه، فكل جه"، وقد يكون مفصول النتائج إن لم يصرح بنتائج تلك الأقيسة كقولنا: "كل ج ب، وكل ب أ، فكل أد، وكل د ه، فكل جه". ومنه قياس الخُلف، ومرجعه إلى قياسين إلخ ما قال المصنف يحلله. قياس الخلف إلخ الخلف إن كان بالفتح فهو بمعنى الوراء، وإن كان بالضم فهو الباطل والمحال، وهو قياس يثبت المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه. وإنما سمي "خلفا" أي باطلا، لا لأنه باطل في نفسه بل لأنه ينتج الباطل على تقدير عدم حقية المطلوب. وقال المحقق الطوسي في "شرح الإشارات" في وجه تسمية هذا القياس بالخلف: إن الخلف اسم للشيء الردي والمحال؛ ولذلك سمي القياس به وهذا التفسير أشبه مما يقال: إنه إنما سمي به؛ لأنه يأتي المطلوب من خلفه، أي من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه واعلم أن قياس الخلف يقابل المستقيم من وجوه، منها: أن المستقيم يتوجه إلى إثبات المطلوب أول الأمر، والخلف لا يتوجه أولا إلى إثبات المطلوب، بل إلى إبطال نقيضه. ومنها: أن المستقيم يتألف من مقدمات مناسبة للمطلوب، والخُلف يشمل على ما يناقض المطلوب. ومنها: أن المستقيم يشترط فيه أن يكون مقدماته مسلّمة في أنفسها، أو ما يجري مجرى التسليم بخلاف الخُلف. ومنها: أن المطلوب في يوضع أولا ثم ينتقل منه إلى نقيضه، وفي المستقيم لا يكون موضوعا أولا حتى يتم تأليفه ويحصل.
Section: Among the compound syllogisms is a syllogism called qiyas al-khalf (syllogism by contradiction/reductio), and its basis is two syllogisms: one is a conjunctive conditional syllogism composed of two conditionals, and the second is an exceptional syllogism one of whose premises is implicative. 'From the syllogisms' etc.: Know that the syllogism that produces the desired conclusion is not composed except of two premises, no more and no less, but to obtain the desired conclusion one may need to acquire another syllogism similarly until the acquisition ends at axiomatic premises; thus there are ordered syllogisms yielding the syllogism that produces the desired conclusion, and this is called a compound syllogism. It may be continuous in its results, i.e., all the results of those syllogisms are explicitly stated, like our saying: 'Every J is B, and every B is A, so every J is A; and every A is D, so every J is D; and every D is H, so every J is H'. It may be discontinuous in its results if the results of those syllogisms are not explicitly stated, like our saying: 'Every J is B, and every B is A, so every A is D; and every D is H, so every J is H'. Among them is qiyas al-khalf, and its basis is two syllogisms as the author said and he analyzes it. 'Qiyas al-khalf' etc.: al-khalf if with fatha means 'behind'; if with damma it means falsehood and impossible. It is a syllogism that proves the desired conclusion by refuting its contradictory. It is called 'khalf' i.e., false, not because it is false in itself, but because it yields falsehood on the assumption that the desired conclusion is not true. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi said in Sharh al-Isharat regarding the reason for naming this syllogism al-khalf: verily khalf is a name for a bad thing and impossible; therefore the syllogism was named by it. This interpretation is more plausible than what is said: that it is named so because it comes to the desired from behind it, i.e., from its opposite which is its contradictory. And know that qiyas al-khalf contrasts with direct syllogism (mustaqim) in several respects: among them, that the direct syllogism proceeds to prove the desired conclusion from the outset, while al-khalf does not proceed first to prove the desired conclusion, but rather to refute its contradictory. Among them, that the direct syllogism is composed of premises appropriate to the desired conclusion, while al-khalf includes what contradicts the desired conclusion. Among them, that in the direct syllogism it is required that its premises be self-evident or treated as conceded, unlike al-khalf. Among them, that in al-khalf the desired conclusion is placed first then one moves from it to its contradictory, whereas in the direct syllogism it is not placed first until its composition is completed and it is obtained.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas al-khalf (syllogism by contradiction or reductio ad absurdum) and explains its structure and characteristics. The author begins by stating that it is a type of compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab), consisting of two syllogisms: a conjunctive conditional syllogism (iqtirani sharti) composed of two conditionals, and an exceptional syllogism (istithna'i) one of whose premises is implicative. The commentary then elaborates on the nature of compound syllogisms: a productive syllogism only has two premises, but to reach the desired conclusion through multiple steps, one may need a series of syllogisms leading back to self-evident premises. This series can be 'continuous in results' (mawsul al-nata'ij) where each intermediate conclusion is explicitly stated, or 'discontinuous' (mafsul) where some results are omitted. Examples are given using Arabic logical variables (J, B, A, D, H). The passage then explains the etymology of 'khalf': with fatha (khalf) it means 'behind'; with damma (khulf) it means falsehood/impossible. The syllogism is called khalf because it yields a contradiction if the desired conclusion is false. Al-Tusi's interpretation is cited: it is named after the 'bad/impossible' outcome. The passage then contrasts qiyas al-khalf with the direct syllogism (mustaqim) in four ways: 1) Direct proceeds to prove the desired conclusion; khalf first refutes its contradictory. 2) Direct uses premises suitable to the conclusion; khalf includes premises that contradict it. 3) Direct requires its premises to be conceded; khalf does not. 4) In khalf, the desired conclusion is placed first then one moves to its contradictory; in direct, the conclusion is not placed first until the composition is complete.

The Syllogism

Qiyas al-Khalf: Proof by Contradiction
Defines qiyas al-khalf and compares it with direct syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a qiyas al-khalf argument and compare it with a direct syllogism (mustaqim).

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves a proposition by showing its negation leads to contradiction.

While direct syllogisms move from conceded premises to the desired conclusion, a powerful alternative is proof by contradiction, called qiyas al-khalf. This lesson explains its structure as a compound of two syllogisms: a conditional and an exceptional. We will also see how it differs from the direct syllogism in purpose, composition, and epistemic assumptions. The name 'khalf' (with damma) means falsehood or impossibility, reflecting that the argument produces an absurd result from the assumption of the negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (matlub)
Start with the proposition you want to prove. For example, 'The world has a creator.'
2
Assume its negation (naqid)
Assume the opposite: 'The world does not have a creator.' This is a temporary assumption to be refuted.
3
Derive a contradiction from the negation
Using conceded premises, show that the assumption leads to an impossible consequence. For instance, 'If the world has no creator, then something temporally originated exists without a cause, which contradicts the principle that everything originated requires a cause.'
This step involves constructing a conditional syllogism linking the assumption to an absurdity.
4
Conclude the negation of the assumption
Since the assumption leads to absurdity, it must be false. Therefore, the original desired conclusion is true (by restitutio ad absurdum).
The exceptional syllogism then denies the absurd consequence, thus affirming the matlub.

Source Text

The Syllogism
intermediate

Reductio ad Absurdum: The Two-Syllogism Structure of Qiyas al-Khalf
Author defines reductio, explains its two-syllogism structure, and illustrates with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two-syllogism structure of qiyas al-khalf and verify its validity using Islamic examples from Fiqh or Kalam.

Core Idea
Reductio proves a claim by showing its negation leads to an absurdity via two linked syllogisms.

In this passage, the author introduces a powerful compound technique widely used in Islamic sciences: reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf). Unlike direct syllogisms that aim to prove a claim straightforwardly, reductio demonstrates the truth of a proposition by exposing the impossibility of its opposite. The author explains that qiyas al-khalf is composed of two sub-syllogisms: a conditional conjunctive syllogism that establishes that the negation of the claim leads to an absurdity, and a conditional exceptive syllogism that, on the basis that the absurd does not occur, concludes the original claim must be true. Understanding this structure is essential for evaluating many arguments in Kalam, Usul al-Fiqh, and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall claim (al-mudda'a)
The reductio aims to prove that a certain proposition P is true. In the passage, the example is 'Every human is an animal'. In Islamic sciences, this could be a theological doctrine or a legal ruling.
The claim is the conclusion that the reductio will eventually establish.
2
Analyse the first sub-syllogism: conditional conjunctive
First, we assume the negation of the claim (not-P). The reductio then shows that not-P leads to an absurdity (a contradiction or an impossible consequence). This is expressed as two conditionals: 'If not-P, then Q' and 'If Q, then absurdity'. From these, we derive the conditional conclusion: 'If not-P, then absurdity'. This is the first syllogism, which is a conditional conjunctive (iqtirani sharṭi).
The author calls this the 'conditional conjunctive syllogism composed of two conditionals'.
3
Analyse the second sub-syllogism: conditional exceptive
The second syllogism takes the conclusion of the first as a premise: 'If not-P, then absurdity'. It then adds the exception: 'But absurdity is not established (i.e., false)'. Using modus tollens, we deduce 'Therefore, not-P is not true', i.e., P is true. This second syllogism is an exceptive (istithnā'ī) syllogism.
The exception is the denial of the consequent, which forces the denial of the antecedent.
4
Apply the structure to a concrete example
Work through the author's example: 'Every human is an animal'. Assume it is false -> 'Some human is not an animal' is true. But that is absurd because a human by definition is a rational animal. So the first syllogism yields: 'If it is false that every human is an animal, then absurdity follows'. Then the second syllogism: 'But absurdity does not obtain; therefore it is not false that every human is an animal; therefore it is true.'
Notice the reductio uses the contradictory of the original claim, not just its negation.
5
Distinguish reductio from direct syllogism
The passage lists four differences: (1) Direct aims to prove the sought directly; reductio aims to refute its contradictory. (2) Direct uses premises appropriate to the sought; reductio includes premises that contradict the sought. (3) Direct requires premises accepted as true; reductio does not. (4) In reductio, the sought is posited first and then we move to its contradictory; in direct, the sought is the conclusion. Understanding these differences helps identify when a reductio is being used.
These contrasts are crucial for correctly classifying arguments in Islamic logical works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: ومن الأقيسة المركبة قياس يسمّى قياس الخُلف، ومرجعه إلى قياسين: أحدهما: اقتراني شرطي مركب من المتصلتين، وثانيهما: استثنائي إحدى مقدمتيه لزومية. ومن الأقيسة إلخ: اعلم أن القياس المنتج المطلوب لا يكون مركبا إلا من مقدمتين، لا أزيد ولا أنقص، لكن قد يحتاج في حصول المطلوب إلى كسب قياس آخر كذلك حتى ينتهي الكسب إلى المقدمات البديهية؛ فيكون هناك قياسات مرتبة محصلة للقياس المنتج للمطلوب، ويسمّى قياسا مركبا، وهو قد يكون موصول النتائج؛ بأن يكون جميع نتائج تلك الأقيسة مصرحة كقولنا: "كل ج ب ، وكل ب أ، فكل ج أ، وكل أد، فكل ج د، وكل د ه، فكل جه"، وقد يكون مفصول النتائج إن لم يصرح بنتائج تلك الأقيسة كقولنا: "كل ج ب، وكل ب أ، فكل أد، وكل د ه، فكل جه". ومنه قياس الخُلف، ومرجعه إلى قياسين إلخ ما قال المصنف يحلله. قياس الخلف إلخ الخلف إن كان بالفتح فهو بمعنى الوراء، وإن كان بالضم فهو الباطل والمحال، وهو قياس يثبت المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه. وإنما سمي "خلفا" أي باطلا، لا لأنه باطل في نفسه بل لأنه ينتج الباطل على تقدير عدم حقية المطلوب. وقال المحقق الطوسي في "شرح الإشارات" في وجه تسمية هذا القياس بالخلف: إن الخلف اسم للشيء الردي والمحال؛ ولذلك سمي القياس به. وهذا التفسير أشبه مما يقال: إنه إنما سمي به؛ لأنه يأتي المطلوب من خلفه، أي من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه. واعلم أن قياس الخلف يقابل المستقيم من وجوه، منها: أن المستقيم يتوجه إلى إثبات المطلوب أول الأمر، والخلف لا يتوجه أولا إلى إثبات المطلوب، بل إلى إبطال نقيضه. ومنها: أن المستقيم يتألف من مقدمات مناسبة للمطلوب، والخُلف يشمل على ما يناقض المطلوب. ومنها: أن المستقيم يشترط فيه أن يكون مقدماته مسلّمة في أنفسها، أو ما يجري مجرى التسليم بخلاف الخُلف. ومنها: أن المطلوب في يوضع أولا ثم ينتقل منه إلى نقيضه، وفي المستقيم لا يكون موضوعا أولا حتى يتم تأليفه ويحصل. أعني نتيجة القياس الأوّل، والمقدمة الأخرى مما استثني فيه نقيض التالي. وتقريره أن يقال : المدعى ثابت؛ لأنه لو لم يثبت المدعى يثبت نقيضه، وكلما يثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال. "لو لم يثبت المدعى ثبت المحال" وهذا أول القياسين. ثم نجعل النتيجة المذكورة صغرى ونقول : "لو لم يثبت المدعى ثبت المحال" ونضم إليه كبرى استثنائيا ونقول: "لكن المحال ليس بثابت فبالضرورة ثبت المدعى وإلا لزم ارتفاع النقيضين". وإن اشتهيت فهم هذا المعنى في مثال جزئي تقول : "كل إنسان حيوان" صادق؛ لأنه لو لم يصدق لصدق بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان، وكلما صدق "بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان" لزم المحال، ينتج "كلما لم يصدق المدعى لزم المحال، لكن المحال ليس بثابت، فعدم ثبوت المدعى ليس بثابت، فالمدعى ثابت".
Section: Among the compound syllogisms is a syllogism called the syllogism of reductio (qiyās al-khalf), and its reference is to two syllogisms: one of them is a conditional conjunctive syllogism composed of two conditionals, and the second is an exceptive syllogism, one of whose premises is the consequence. [Commentary:] 'And from the syllogisms etc.': Know that the productive syllogism that is sought is not compound except from two premises, no more and no less, but sometimes in obtaining the sought one needs to gain another syllogism similarly until the gaining reaches the intuitive premises; then there are ordered syllogisms that result in the syllogism producing the sought, and it is called a compound syllogism. It may be connected in its results, meaning that all the results of those syllogisms are explicitly stated, like our saying: 'Every J is B, and every B is A, so every J is A, and every A is D, so every J is D, and every D is H, so every J is H'; and it may be disconnected in its results if the results of those syllogisms are not explicitly stated, like our saying: 'Every J is B, and every B is A, so every A is D, and every D is H, so every J is H.' And among them is the syllogism of reductio, and its reference is to two syllogisms etc. as the author says, analyzing it. 'The syllogism of reductio etc.' Al-Khalf: if with fatha it means 'behind', and if with damma it means 'false and impossible', and it is a syllogism that proves the sought by refuting its contradictory. It is called 'khalf' meaning false, not because it is false in itself, but because it produces falsehood on the assumption that the sought is not true. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi said in his commentary on al-Isharat regarding the reason for naming this syllogism 'khalf': Indeed, khalf is a name for the bad and impossible thing; therefore the syllogism is named after it. And this interpretation is more akin to what is said: It is named thus because it comes to the sought from behind it, i.e., from its rear which is its contradictory. And know that the syllogism of reductio contrasts with the direct syllogism in several respects: among them, the direct syllogism aims at establishing the sought at the outset, while the reductio does not aim at establishing the sought initially, but at refuting its contradictory. Among them, the direct syllogism is composed of premises appropriate to the sought, while the reductio includes what contradicts the sought. Among them, for the direct syllogism it is required that its premises be accepted in themselves, or what is like acceptance, unlike the reductio. Among them, the sought in [the reductio] is placed first and then one moves to its contradictory, while in the direct [syllogism] it is not placed first until its composition is completed and obtained. [The second syllogism:] I mean the result of the first syllogism, and the other premise which is the excepted consequent of the conditional. Its formulation is that one says: The claim is established; because if the claim were not established, its contradictory would be established, and whenever its contradictory is established, the absurd is established. 'If the claim were not established, the absurd would be established' — this is the first of the two syllogisms. Then we make the mentioned result the minor premise and say: 'If the claim were not established, the absurd would be established', and we join to it a major premise as an exceptive and say: 'But the absurd is not established, so necessarily the claim is established, otherwise the negation of the two contradictories would follow.' And if you desire to understand this meaning in a particular example, you say: 'Every human is an animal' is true; because if it were not true, then 'Some human is not an animal' would be true, and whenever 'Some human is not an animal' is true, the absurd follows. It produces: 'Whenever the claim is not true, the absurd follows; but the absurd is not established; so the non-establishment of the claim is not established; so the claim is established.'
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the qiyas al-khalf (syllogism of reductio ad absurdum). The author first places it within the category of compound syllogisms, noting that a compound syllogism is needed when the desired conclusion requires more than one syllogism to reach intuitive premises. The compound syllogism may have its intermediate results explicitly stated (connected) or not (disconnected). The reductio is a special type of compound syllogism that reduces to two syllogisms: a conditional conjunctive syllogism composed of two conditionals, and a conditional exceptive syllogism (istithna'i) whose second premise is the consequent of the conditional. The author then discusses the etymology of 'khalf': with fatha it means 'behind', with damma 'false/impossible'. The syllogism is named 'khalf' (false) because it proves the sought by showing that its negation leads to an impossibility or absurdity. Al-Tusi's interpretation is preferred: the name comes from the absurdity produced on the assumption that the sought is false. The reductio is contrasted with the direct syllogism (mustaqim) in four ways: (1) direct aims to prove the sought directly; reductio aims to refute its contradictory. (2) direct uses premises appropriate to the sought; reductio includes premises that contradict the sought. (3) direct requires premises that are accepted as true; reductio does not require that. (4) in reductio, the sought is posited first and then one moves to its contradictory; in direct, the sought is not posited until the end. Next, the structure is explicated: the first syllogism is conditional: 'If the claim is not established, then its contradictory is established, and whenever the contradictory is established, the absurd is established.' The result is 'If the claim is not established, the absurd is established.' This result then becomes the minor premise of the second syllogism: 'If the claim is not established, the absurd is established; but the absurd is not established; therefore, the claim is established.' The example given is the proposition 'Every human is an animal.' If this were false, then 'Some human is not an animal' would be true, leading to absurdity (since that contradicts the definition of human), so the original proposition must be true. The author uses logical variables (J, B, A, D, H) in the earlier explanation but then gives a concrete example from metaphysics (human and animal).

Key Terms
قياس الخلف (qiyās al-khalf): A syllogism that proves a claim by showing that its negation leads to an absurdity (impossible or contradictory conclusion). It consists of two sub-syllogisms: a conditional conjunctive syllogism and a conditional exceptive syllogism. [Note: In later texts, qiyas al-khalf is often treated as a single syllogism rather than a compound of two. The structure given here is specific to the sharh tradition.]
القياس المركب (al-qiyās al-murakkab): A compound syllogism made up of multiple individual syllogisms whose conclusions serve as premises for subsequent syllogisms, ultimately reaching intuitive premises. [Note: Stable term; though some later logicians may distinguish between 'murakkab' and 'muttasil' (connected) types.]
الاقتراني الشرطي (al-iqtrānī al-sharṭī): A conjunctive syllogism composed of two conditional premises. Here it refers to the first part of the reductio, which combines two conditionals to derive a conditional conclusion. [Note: In standard logic, 'iqtirani' usually refers to categorical syllogisms; here it is used for conditional conjunctive.]
الاستثنائي (al-istithnā'ī): An exceptive syllogism, where one premise is a conditional and the other is the exception (affirmation or negation) of one of its parts. Here it is the second syllogism that affirms the claim by denying the consequent. [Note: Stable term across logical tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio syllogism (qiyas al-khalf), what is the purpose of the first of the two sub-syllogisms?
A) To directly prove the desired conclusion.
B) To establish that the negation of the claim leads to an absurdity.
C) To show that the premises are accepted by the opponent.
D) To convert a universal proposition into a particular one.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a valid compound syllogism that proves a claim by demonstrating that its opposite leads to an impossible consequence.

Syllogistic Form and Matter
Author defines form and matter of syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the form and matter of a syllogism and explain why both are necessary for a valid argument.

Core Idea
Every syllogism has an arrangement (form) and content (matter); validity depends on both.

The author reminds us that every syllogism necessarily has two dimensions: form and matter. Form is the arrangement of premises, which you have already studied in the four figures. Matter is the actual content of those premises—their epistemic quality. This lesson focuses on form and matter as a foundational concept before diving into the classification of premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two essential components
The passage states that every syllogism must have form and matter. Form is the configuration from arranging premises. Matter is the premises themselves.
Form is about structure; matter is about content.
2
Recall the four productive figures
The author says you already know the four figures and their conditions. Form is what makes a syllogism valid or invalid in terms of arrangement.
You have covered this earlier—now focus on matter.
3
Define 'matter' in your own words
Matter is not just any premises, but premises classified by how they are known (epistemic source). The author will now explain this classification.
4
Explain why both are necessary
A syllogism can have perfect form (valid figure and mood) but if its matter is weak (e.g., dubious premises), the conclusion is not reliable. Conversely, certain matter in a flawed form yields reliable conclusion? No, form must also be valid.
Both dimensions must be sound for the syllogism to be epistemically valuable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: ينبغي أن يعلم أن كل قياس لا بد له من صورة ومادة. أما الصورة فهو الهيأة الحاصلة من ترتيب المقدمات ووضع بعضها عند بعض. وقد عرفت الأشكال الأربعة المنتجة، وعلمت شرائطها في الإنتاج بقي أمر المادة. والقدماء حتى الشيخ الرئيس المادة، وهي ينبغي أن يعلم إلخ لما فرغ المصنف بحاله عن مباحث الحجة من حيث الصورة أراد أن يبين أحوالها من جهة القضايا التي تتركب منها ولنبدأ بتفسير المبادئ القضايا التي ينتهي إليها الأقيسة إما أن تفيد تصديقا أو تأثيرا آخر غير التصديق الثانية القضايا المخيلة، وما يفيد تصديقا فإما يفيد الظن فهي المظنونات، أو يقينا، فإما يقينا جازما مطابقا للواقع من حيث إنها مطابقة فهي الواجب قبولها، أو يقينا من جهة الشهرة بين الجمهور فهي المشهورات أو من جهة تسليم إمام يوثق به فهي المقبولات، أو من جهة تسليم أحد المتخاصمين فهي المسلّمات، أو من جهة مشابهته للصوادق أو المشهورات فهي المشبهات أو من جهة حكم الوهم الوهميات. وما لا يفيد تصديقا ولا تأثيرا آخر فلا اعتداد له عند أصحاب الصناعات كالمشكوكات مثلا.
Section: It should be known that every syllogism necessarily has a form and a matter. As for form, it is the configuration resulting from the arrangement of the premises and placing some next to others. You have already known the four productive figures, and you have learned their conditions for production; the matter remains. The ancients, even the Chief (Ibn Sina) ... (commentary) ... the premises to which syllogisms ultimately reduce either produce assent (tasdiq) or another effect besides assent, namely the imaginative premises. What produces assent either produces opinion, then they are the opined; or certainty, then either certain certainty conforming to reality in that they conform, then they are the ones that must be accepted; or certainty due to notoriety among the public, then they are the renowned; or due to the acceptance of a trustworthy imam, then they are the accepted; or due to the acceptance of one of the disputants, then they are the conceded; or due to their similarity to truths or renowned, then they are the resembling; or due to the judgment of the estimative faculty, then they are the estimative. And what does not produce assent nor another effect is not considered by the practitioners of the arts, like the dubious, for example.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the two essential aspects of every syllogism: form (صورة) and matter (مادة). Form is the arrangement of premises; matter is the content. The author notes that the four figures and their conditions have been covered, so now the focus shifts to matter. He then provides a hierarchical classification of premises (مبادئ القضايا) that constitute the matter. Premises are first divided into those producing assent (تصديق) and those producing another effect, which is imaginative (تخييل), relevant to poetry. Premises that produce assent are further divided into those producing opinion (ظن) and those producing certainty (يقين). Certainty is subdivided into: absolute certainty conforming to reality, and certainty based on notoriety (شهرة), acceptance of an authority (قبول), concession in dialectic (تسليم), similarity to truth or notoriety (تشبيه), or estimative judgment (وهم). Premises that produce neither assent nor any other effect, such as dubious propositions (مشكوكات), are disregarded in the arts. This classification is foundational for distinguishing the five logical arts (صناعات خمس) which are discussed later.

Key Terms
صورة القياس
مادة القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'form' (ṣūra) of a syllogism refer to?
A) The truth value of the premises
B) The configuration resulting from the arrangement of premises
C) The subject matter of the premises
D) The number of premises

Key Takeaway
Every syllogism has two necessary dimensions: form (arrangement of premises) and matter (the premises themselves); both affect the syllogism's value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Classification of Premises by Epistemic Value
Author classifies premises by their epistemic effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any premise according to its epistemic type as described by the author (yaqini, mashhuri, maqbul, musallam, mushabbih, wahmi, maznun, mukhayyil, mashkuk) and explain how each type is used in different logical arts.

Core Idea
Premises are classified by the certainty they produce: from certain (yaqini) to imaginative (mukhayyil) to useless (mashkuk).

After establishing that matter refers to the premises, the author provides a detailed classification of premises based on the effect they produce in the mind. Some produce assent (tasdiq), some produce imagination (takhayyul), and some produce nothing. Among those producing assent, some yield opinion (zann) and some yield certainty (yaqin). Certainty itself has several sources: direct correspondence to reality (yaqini), public notoriety (mashhuri), acceptance from a trustworthy authority (maqbul), concession in debate (musallam), similarity to truth or notoriety (mushabbih), and estimative judgment (wahmi). This classification is crucial for understanding which premises are appropriate for demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), rhetoric (khataba), poetry (shi'r), and sophistry (mughalata).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the primary division: assent vs. other effect
The passage divides premises into those that produce assent (tasdiq) and those that produce another effect—imagination (takhayyul). Premises that produce no effect (like dubious) are disregarded.
Assent is acceptance as true; imagination is not belief but mental imagery (used in poetry).
2
Identify the two subcategories under assent: opinion and certainty
Within assent, some premises produce only opinion (maznunat) and some produce certainty (yaqiniyyat). Opinion is weaker, certainty is stronger.
Opinion is probable, certainty is decisive.
3
List the six subtypes of certainty premises
The author enumerates six types: (1) yaqini (certain conforming to reality), (2) mashhuri (renowned), (3) maqbul (accepted from an authority), (4) musallam (conceded by an opponent), (5) mushabbih (resembling truth or renown), (6) wahmi (based on estimative faculty).
Each type has a different epistemic source.
4
Recognize the role of these premises in different arts
The yaqini are used in demonstration (burhan), mashhuri and musallam in dialectic (jadal), maqbul in rhetoric (khataba), mushabbih in sophistry (mughalata), and mukhayyil in poetry (shi'r). Wahmi are often used in sophistry since they appear certain but are false.
The classification directly relates to the five logical arts.
